Definition of the Third Circle (First Heavens)
Defines the third circle (circle of the first heavens).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition, naming rationale, and key properties of the third circle, known as the circle of the first heavens.

Core Idea
A star on this circle has no azimuth – that's why it's special.

In this passage, the author introduces the third circle of the celestial sphere. This circle is unique because a star positioned on it has no azimuth (samt), a technical term you will learn later. It is also called the circle of the east and west because it passes through those two cardinal points. Understanding this circle is essential for grasping how directions are measured on the celestial sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the full context including the headings.
The headings indicate this is part of a larger discussion on directions of a body.
2
Identify the two names of the circle
The passage gives two names for this circle. Find them and note the reason for each name.
One name relates to the first heavens, the other to east and west.
3
Understand the key property: no azimuth
The passage states that a star on this circle has no azimuth. Think about what that implies for direction measurement.
Azimuth is a direction along the horizon – this circle is vertical.
4
Learn what the circle separates and its poles
The circle separates the northern and southern halves of the sphere. Its poles are the north and south points on the horizon.
The poles are where the circle intersects the meridian.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني: القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم الدائرة الثالثة ( دائرة أول السماوات ) لأن الكوكب إذا كان على هذه الدائرة لم يكن له سمت كما ستعرفه وتسمى أيضاً دائرة المشرق والمغرب لمرورها بنقطتيهما ( وتفصل ) هذه الدائرة ( بين النصف الشمالي والنصف الجنوبي من الفلك وقطباها نقطتا الشمال والجنوب من الأفق ) أعني نقطتي تقاطعه مع نصف النهار
The first observation - the second chapter: the first section: the second intention: on the directions of the body. The third circle (the circle of the first heavens) because if a star is on this circle, it has no azimuth (samt) as you will know. It is also called the circle of the east and west because it passes through the two points. And this circle separates between the northern and southern halves of the sphere, and its poles are the points of north and south on the horizon, i.e., the points of its intersection with the meridian.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the third circle, called 'circle of the first heavens' (dā'irat ūlā al-samāwāt) or 'circle of the east and west'. The author explains that a star on this circle has no azimuth (samt) — a term for the direction along the horizon to the star's vertical circle. This circle passes through the east and west points (intersections of horizon and equator) and separates the northern and southern halves of the sphere. Its poles are the north and south points on the horizon, which are the intersections of the horizon with the meridian.

Key Terms
دائرة أول السماوات
دائرة المشرق والمغرب
السمت
النصف الشمالي
النصف الجنوبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The east-west circle is the same as the horizon.
Correction: The east-west circle is a different great circle that passes through the east and west points, whereas the horizon is the circle where the sky meets the ground.

Checkpoint
Why is the third circle called 'circle of the first heavens' according to the passage?
A) Because it is the highest circle
B) Because a star on it has no azimuth (samt)
C) Because it passes through the poles of the equator
D) Because it separates day and night

Key Takeaway
The third circle is defined by the property that a star on it has no azimuth; it separates the northern and southern celestial hemispheres and has its poles at the north and south points on the horizon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage mentions that a star on this circle has no azimuth (direction). When in your life have you felt directionless? How does the knowledge that Allah is the source of all guidance give you hope and direction even when you are confused? Identify one specific step you can take today to turn to Him more consciously.
?
This circle separates the northern and southern halves of the sphere. Consider how Allah has set clear boundaries and separations in creation (e.g., day and night, land and sea). Are there boundaries in your own life that you have blurred or neglected? What practical change can you make to restore the clarity He has ordained for you?

The Azimuth Circle and Its Constant Perpendicularity
Defines the azimuth circle and states its perpendicular property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the azimuth circle and why it always cuts the ecliptic at right angles.

Core Idea
The azimuth circle always cuts the ecliptic perpendicularly because it passes through both sets of poles.

The fourth circle is introduced by its defining property: it passes through the poles of the horizon and the ecliptic. This unique feature ensures it always intersects the ecliptic at right angles, unlike the meridian. Let's explore this circle and its names.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the fourth circle
Read the Arabic passage defining the circle. Notice that it passes through the poles of both the horizon and the ecliptic.
2
Identify the names of this circle
The circle is called the azimuth circle, the circle of the latitude of the region of visibility, and the circle of the middle of the sky of visibility. Understand why each name is used.
The names reflect its functions and properties.
3
Compare with the meridian
The passage states that this circle is always perpendicular to the ecliptic, unlike the meridian. Ask yourself why the meridian sometimes cuts obliquely.
This contrast highlights the unique property of the azimuth circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابعة : ) هذه الخمس ( تمر بقطبي الأفق وبقطبي المنطقة فتكون أبداً مقاطعة لهما على قوائم بخلاف نصف النهار، فإنها قد تقطع المنطقة لا على زوايا قوائم ( وتسمى ) هذه الدائرة ( دائرة السمت و ) دائرة (عرض) إقليم الرؤية لأن القوس الواقعة منها بين الأفق وقطب منطقة البروج أو بين قطب الأفق ومنطقة البروج تسمى عرض إقليم الرؤية (و) تسمى أيضاً دائرة ( وسط سماء الرؤية لأنها تفصل بين نصفي فلك الثوابت وفيه
من
قوله : ( وعرض إقليم الرؤية) تشبيهاً له بعرض البلد .
قوله : ( فهو سماء الرؤية ) ولهذا سمي إقليم الرؤية.
قوله : (قد تقطع المنطقة لا على زوايا قوائم وذلك إذا لم ينطبق دائرة نصف النهار على المارة بالأقطاب اعلم أن المارة بالأقطاب تكون منطبقة على نصف النهار في كل يوم بليلته مرتين وبيانه إذا تحرك الفلك الأعلى بالحركة اليومية تحرك قطبا المنطقة على قطبي المعدل وتحرك أيضاً الدائرة المارة تبعاً لحركة قطبي المنطقة فإذا وصل هذان القطبان إلى جزأين من دائرة نصف النهار أحدهما ، أي أحد الجزأين تحت الأفق والآخر ،فوقه كانت المارة ح منطبقة على نصف النهار وكانت هذه الدائرة أعني دائرة السمت منطبقة على نصف النهار وكانت هذه الدائرة أعني دائرة السمت منطبقة على نصف النهار أيضاً فح كان نصف النهار مقاطعاً للمنطقة على زوايا قوائم فإذا زال القطبان عن ذينك الجزأين من دائرة نصف النهار ولم يكن نصف النهار حينئذ قاطعاً للمنطقة على زوايا قوائم إذ هو حينئذ لم يكن ماراً بالقطبين وكل دائرة عظيمة مقاطعة للأخرى إذا لم يكن مارة بقطبيها كان تقاطعهما لا على زوايا قائمة .
منطقة
قوله : ( بين الأفق وقطب منطقة البروج أي إذا وقع قطب المنطقة على جزء من الأفق في جانب الجنوب مثلاً . لم يتصور هناك قوس بين الأفق وبين قطب المنطقة إذ المفروض أنه لم يقع بينهما المفارقة ،بعد ، ثم إذا فارق قطب المنطقة بالحركة اليومية عن ذلك الجزء من الأفق يتصور القوس بين الأفق وبين قطب المنطقة وكذا إذا كان قطب أفق من الآفاق واقعاً على جزء من البروج لم يتصور هناك قوس بين ذلك الأفق وبين منطقة البروج وهو ظاهر. أما إذا كان قطب أفق الآفاق واقعاً على جزء من سطح الفلك غير جزء منطقة البروج فيتصور هناك قوس بين قطب الأفق ومنطقة البروج إذا عرفت هذا فالقوس الأصغر الواقع من دائرة السمت من قطب المنطقة وبين الجزء الأقرب إليه من أجزاء الأفق يسمى عرض إقليم الرؤية دون القوس الأطول منها بين الجزء الأبعد وبين ذلك القطب وكذا القوس الواقع منها بين قطب الأفق وبين الجزء الأقرب إليه من أجزاء المنطقة عرض إقليم الرؤية أيضاً كما ذكره فتأمل
And the fourth: these five pass through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the ecliptic, so they are always perpendicular to them, unlike the meridian, for it may cut the ecliptic not at right angles. And this circle is called the azimuth circle and the circle of the latitude of the region of visibility, because the arc of it between the horizon and the pole of the ecliptic, or between the pole of the horizon and the ecliptic, is called the latitude of the region of visibility. And it is also called the circle of the middle of the sky of visibility because it separates the two halves of the sphere of fixed stars. And therein [commentary]: His saying: 'and the latitude of the region of visibility' likening it to the latitude of a town. His saying: 'so it is the sky of visibility' and therefore it is called the region of visibility. His saying: 'it may cut the ecliptic not at right angles, and that is when the meridian does not coincide with the circle passing through the poles. Know that the circle passing through the poles coincides with the meridian twice each day and night. Its explanation: when the highest sphere moves with the daily motion, the poles of the ecliptic move on the poles of the equator, and the circle passing through the poles also moves following the motion of the poles of the ecliptic. When these two poles reach two parts of the meridian, one of them below the horizon and the other above it, then the circle passing through the poles coincides with the meridian, and this circle, i.e., the azimuth circle, also coincides with the meridian. At that time, the meridian cuts the ecliptic at right angles. When the poles depart from those two parts of the meridian, then the meridian does not cut the ecliptic at right angles, because it is not passing through the poles. And any great circle that cuts another if it does not pass through its poles, their intersection is not at right angles. His saying: 'between the horizon and the pole of the ecliptic' meaning if the pole of the ecliptic lies on a part of the horizon in the south side, for example, no arc is conceived between the horizon and the pole of the ecliptic since it is assumed that no separation has occurred between them. Then when the pole of the ecliptic departs by the daily motion from that part of the horizon, an arc is conceived between the horizon and the pole of the ecliptic. Likewise, if the pole of a horizon lies on a part of the ecliptic, no arc is conceived between that horizon and the ecliptic, which is clear. But if the pole of a horizon lies on a part of the surface of the sphere other than the part of the ecliptic, then an arc is conceived between the pole of the horizon and the ecliptic. If you understand this, then the smaller arc of the azimuth circle from the pole of the ecliptic to the nearest part of the horizon is called the latitude of the region of visibility, not the longer arc from the farther part to that pole. Likewise, the arc from the pole of the horizon to the nearest part of the ecliptic is also called the latitude of the region of visibility, as mentioned. So ponder.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth circle, the azimuth circle (dā'irat al-samt), also called the circle of the latitude of the region of visibility (ʿarḍ iqlīm al-ruʾya) and the circle of the middle of the sky of visibility. The author explains that this circle always passes through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the ecliptic, thus it always cuts the ecliptic at right angles, unlike the meridian which cuts at right angles only when it coincides with the azimuth circle. The term 'ʿarḍ iqlīm al-ruʾya' (latitude of the region of visibility) is defined as the arc along the azimuth circle from the horizon to the pole of the ecliptic (or from the pole of the horizon to the ecliptic), whichever is smaller. The commentary then explains in detail the condition for the meridian to cut the ecliptic at right angles: when the poles of the ecliptic lie exactly on the meridian (i.e., the azimuth circle coincides with the meridian). It also clarifies how the arc of the latitude is defined, emphasizing that it is the smaller arc, not the larger one.

Key Terms
دائرة السمت
منطقة البروج
قطب الأفق
قطب المنطقة
زوايا قوائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The meridian always cuts the ecliptic at right angles.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the meridian may cut the ecliptic not at right angles, unlike the azimuth circle which always does.

Checkpoint
What is unique about the azimuth circle compared to the meridian?
A) It always cuts the ecliptic at right angles.
B) It only cuts the ecliptic at right angles twice daily.
C) It passes only through the poles of the ecliptic.
D) It is the same as the meridian.

Key Takeaway
The azimuth circle is defined by its constant perpendicularity to the ecliptic, a property it holds because it passes through both poles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The azimuth circle always aligns perfectly with the two sets of poles, never deviating. In your life, are there two 'poles' (like truth and action, or knowledge and practice) that you must constantly align with? Where do you allow yourself to cut obliquely, compromising one for the other? What will you do today to bring your actions into 'right angle' alignment with the truth you know?
?
The passage points out that the meridian may cut the ecliptic at non-right angles, yet many assume it is always perpendicular. What assumptions do you hold about your own spiritual state that may be similarly mistaken? Are you trusting something to be aligned with the truth when it might not be? How will you test it using a criterion like the 'azimuth circle' of revelation?

Latitude of Visibility and Meridian's Condition
Explains the latitude arc and the condition for meridian perpendicularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'latitude of the region of visibility' and the condition for the meridian to cut the ecliptic perpendicularly.

Core Idea
The latitude of visibility is the smallest arc along the azimuth circle; the meridian only cuts the ecliptic perpendicularly twice daily.

The commentary now delves deeper into two topics: the precise definition of the arc called 'latitude of the region of visibility', and the geometric condition under which the meridian becomes perpendicular to the ecliptic. Both rely on the motion of the poles and the coincidence of circles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of the latitude arc
The passage defines the latitude of the region of visibility as the smaller arc along the azimuth circle between the pole of the ecliptic and the horizon (or between the pole of the horizon and the ecliptic). Note that it is the smaller arc, not the larger one.
This is analogous to the latitude of a town on Earth.
2
Grasp the condition for perpendicular intersection of the meridian
The commentary explains that the meridian cuts the ecliptic at right angles only when it coincides with the circle passing through the poles (i.e., the azimuth circle). This happens twice daily when the poles of the ecliptic lie exactly on the meridian.
This resolves why the meridian is not always perpendicular.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider how these definitions and conditions are used in practical astronomy, such as determining visibility of celestial objects.

منطقة

This passage defines the fourth circle, the azimuth circle (dā'irat al-samt), also called the circle of the latitude of the region of visibility (ʿarḍ iqlīm al-ruʾya) and the circle of the middle of the sky of visibility. Under what condition does the meridian cut the ecliptic at right angles?
A) Always, because it is a great circle.
B) When the azimuth circle coincides with the meridian.
C) Only at noon.
D) When the sun is at the equator.

Key Takeaway
The latitude of the region of visibility is defined as the smaller arc along the azimuth circle, and the meridian's perpendicularity to the ecliptic depends on its coincidence with the azimuth circle twice daily.

The Third Circle: First Heaven Circle
Definition of the third circle with naming and properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition, naming, function, and poles of the third circle (first heaven circle) in Islamic astronomy.

Core Idea
The first heaven circle defines the celestial division through east-west axis.

This lesson introduces the third celestial circle, known as the circle of the first heaven. It is a fundamental concept for understanding the directions of celestial bodies. The passage explains its names, its role in separating the northern and southern hemispheres, and its poles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three main parts: naming, function, and poles.
The passage is concise; each sentence adds a distinct piece of information.
2
Identify the names and their reasons
Find why it is called 'circle of the first heaven' and 'circle of east and west'. Understand that a planet on this circle has no azimuth.
3
Understand its function
The passage states it separates the northern and southern halves of the celestial sphere. Visualize this division.
4
Locate its poles
The poles are the north and south points on the horizon, which are the intersections of the horizon with the meridian.
The meridian (niṣf al-nahār) is the circle passing through the zenith and north-south points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني: القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم الدائرة الثالثة ( دائرة أول السماوات ) لأن الكوكب إذا كان على هذه الدائرة لم يكن له سمت كما ستعرفه وتسمى أيضاً دائرة المشرق والمغرب لمرورها بنقطتيهما ( وتفصل ) هذه الدائرة ( بين النصف الشمالي والنصف الجنوبي من الفلك وقطباها نقطتا الشمال والجنوب من الأفق ) أعني نقطتي تقاطعه مع نصف النهار .
The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Section One: Second Purpose: On the Directions of the Body. The third circle (the circle of the first heaven) because if a planet is on this circle, it has no azimuth (samt) as you will know, and it is also called the circle of the east and west because it passes through their two points. And this circle separates between the northern half and the southern half of the celestial sphere, and its poles are the points of north and south on the horizon, meaning the two points of its intersection with the meridian (niṣf al-nahār).
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the third circle, known as the circle of the first heaven (dā'irat awwal al-samāwāt). The definition proceeds in sequence: first it gives the name and the reason for the name—if a planet lies on this circle, it has no azimuth (samt). Then it gives another name (circle of the east and west) because it passes through the east and west points. After naming, it describes the circle's function: it separates the celestial sphere into northern and southern halves. Finally, it specifies its poles: the north and south points on the horizon, which are the intersection points of the horizon with the meridian. The passage is a straightforward definition with naming and descriptive details.

The Fourth Circle: Circle of Azimuth
Defines the fourth circle and lists its three alternative names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition, geometric property, and three alternative names of the fourth circle (circle of azimuth) in Islamic astronomy.

Core Idea
The fourth circle always cuts the horizon and zodiac at right angles – unlike the meridian.

In this passage, ʿAḍud al-Dīn al-Ījī introduces the fourth of the five circles used in astronomy. He describes its unique property of intersecting both the horizon and the zodiac at right angles, and gives three names that reflect its different functions. Understanding this circle is key to grasping how celestial coordinates are mapped for visibility calculations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
Notice the broken ending 'wafīhi' suggests continuation.
2
Identify the key property
The text states that this circle always intersects the horizon and zodiac at right angles. Contrast this with the meridian, which may cut the zodiac obliquely.
This perpendicularity is crucial for its function.
3
List the three names
The circle is called: (1) circle of azimuth, (2) circle of latitude of the region of visibility, (3) circle of the middle of the heaven of visibility. Understand why each name is fitting.
Each name highlights a different aspect of its role.
4
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The passage ends with 'wafīhi' (and in it...). Consider what might follow: likely a further description of its function regarding the fixed stars.
This is a typical matn style, leaving details for the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والرابعة : ) هذه الخمس ) تمر بقطبي الأفق وبقطبي المنطقة فتكون أبداً مقاطعة لهما على قوائم بخلاف نصف النهار، فإنها قد تقطع المنطقة لا على زوايا قوائم ( وتسمى ) هذه الدائرة ( دائرة السمت و ) دائرة (عرض) إقليم الرؤية لأن القوس الواقعة منها بين الأفق وقطب منطقة البروج أو بين قطب الأفق ومنطقة البروج تسمى عرض إقليم الرؤية (و) تسمى أيضاً دائرة ( وسط سماء الرؤية لأنها تفصل بين نصفي فلك الثوابت وفيه
And the fourth: (these five) passes through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the zodiac (al-minṭaqa), so it always intersects them at right angles, unlike the meridian, for it may cut the zodiac not at right angles. And this circle is called the circle of azimuth (dā'irat al-samt) and the circle of the latitude of the region of visibility (ʿarḍ iqlīm al-ruʾya) because the arc from it between the horizon and the pole of the zodiac or between the pole of the horizon and the zodiac is called the latitude of the region of visibility. And it is also called the circle of the middle of the heaven of visibility because it separates between the two halves of the sphere of the fixed stars, and in it...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth circle. It begins by stating that it passes through the poles of the horizon and the poles of the zodiac, and therefore always intersects both at right angles—unlike the meridian, which may intersect the zodiac obliquely. Then it gives three names for this circle: (1) circle of azimuth (dā'irat al-samt), (2) circle of the latitude of the region of visibility (ʿarḍ iqlīm al-ruʾya) because the arc from it between the horizon and the pole of the zodiac (or between the horizon's pole and the zodiac) is called the latitude of the region of visibility, and (3) circle of the middle of the heaven of visibility because it separates the two halves of the sphere of the fixed stars. The passage ends abruptly with 'and in it...' (wafīhi), suggesting continuation.

Proof: Fourth Circle Always Perpendicular
Proves why the fourth circle always cuts the horizon and zodiac at right angles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth circle (circle of azimuth) always intersects the horizon and zodiac at right angles, while the meridian does so only twice daily.

Core Idea
The fourth circle always passes through the poles of both the horizon and zodiac, so it always cuts them at right angles.

This lesson explains a key astronomical proof: why the circle of azimuth (the fourth circle) always cuts the horizon and zodiac at perpendicular angles. The passage contrasts it with the meridian, which only does so at specific times. Through tracking the motion of the zodiac's poles, we see the geometric necessity of the fourth circle's constant perpendicularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
The passage contains some technical terms.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage aims to prove that the fourth circle always cuts at right angles. Find the sentence that states this conclusion.
Look at the final principle about great circles.
3
Follow the motion explanation
Trace how the highest sphere's daily motion moves the zodiac's poles, which causes the circle through the poles to coincide with the meridian twice daily.
This explains why the meridian sometimes cuts at right angles.
4
Understand the condition for perpendicularity
The passage states that a great circle intersecting another does so at right angles only if it passes through the other's poles. Apply this to the fourth circle.
The fourth circle always passes through both sets of poles.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many assume the meridian always cuts at right angles. This passage shows it does not, because it doesn't always pass through the zodiac's poles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قد تقطع المنطقة لا على زوايا قوائم وذلك إذا لم ينطبق دائرة نصف النهار على المارة بالأقطاب اعلم أن المارة بالأقطاب تكون منطبقة على نصف النهار في كل يوم بليلته مرتين وبيانه إذا تحرك الفلك الأعلى بالحركة اليومية تحرك قطبا المنطقة على قطبي المعدل وتحرك أيضاً الدائرة المارة تبعاً لحركة قطبي المنطقة فإذا وصل هذان القطبان إلى جزأين من دائرة نصف النهار أحدهما ، أي أحد الجزأين تحت الأفق والآخر ،فوقه كانت المارة ح منطبقة على نصف النهار وكانت هذه الدائرة أعني دائرة السمت منطبقة على نصف النهار وكانت هذه الدائرة أعني دائرة السمت منطبقة على نصف النهار أيضاً فح كان نصف النهار مقاطعاً للمنطقة على زوايا قوائم فإذا زال القطبان عن ذينك الجزأين من دائرة نصف النهار ولم يكن نصف النهار حينئذ قاطعاً للمنطقة على زوايا قوائم إذ هو حينئذ لم يكن ماراً بالقطبين وكل دائرة عظيمة مقاطعة للأخرى إذا لم يكن مارة بقطبيها كان تقاطعهما لا على زوايا قائمة .
His saying: '(It) may cut the zodiac not at right angles, and that is when the meridian does not coincide with the circle passing through the poles. Know that the circle passing through the poles coincides with the meridian twice every day and night. Its explanation: when the highest sphere moves by the daily motion, the poles of the zodiac move on the poles of the equator, and the circle passing through the poles also moves following the motion of the poles of the zodiac. When these two poles reach two parts of the meridian, one of them—i.e., one of the two parts below the horizon and the other above it—then the circle passing through the poles coincides with the meridian, and this circle, I mean the circle of azimuth, also coincides with the meridian. At that time the meridian cuts the zodiac at right angles. When the two poles depart from those two parts of the meridian, then the meridian does not cut the zodiac at right angles, because it no longer passes through the two poles. And any great circle intersecting another, if it does not pass through its two poles, their intersection is not at right angles.'
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the fourth circle (circle of azimuth) always intersects the horizon and zodiac at right angles, whereas the meridian only does so at certain times. The explanation follows the author's own order: It begins by contrasting with the meridian, stating that the circle passing through the poles (the fourth circle) always coincides with the meridian twice a day. The reasoning is astronomical: as the highest sphere rotates daily, the poles of the zodiac move on the poles of the celestial equator, and the circle through the poles follows. When the zodiac's poles reach the meridian (one below horizon, one above), the circle through the poles coincides with the meridian, making the meridian cut the zodiac at right angles. When they move away, the meridian no longer passes through those poles and thus no longer cuts at right angles. The key principle is stated at the end: any great circle intersecting another does so at right angles only if it passes through the other's poles. Since the fourth circle always passes through the poles of both the horizon and the zodiac, it always cuts at right angles.

Key Terms
المارة بالأقطاب
منطقة
نصف النهار
زوايا قوائم
فلك الأعلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The meridian always cuts the zodiac at right angles.
Correction: The meridian only cuts the zodiac at right angles twice daily when the circle through the poles coincides with it; otherwise it does not, because it does not always pass through the zodiac's poles.

Checkpoint

A) Twice a day when the circle through the poles coincides with it
B) Always
C) Never
D) Only at equinoxes

Key Takeaway
The fourth circle's constant perpendicularity follows from the geometric principle that a great circle cutting another at right angles must pass through the other's poles, which the fourth circle always does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider that the meridian only achieves right-angle cuts when it perfectly aligns with the circle through the poles. ?

Think of the 'poles' as the core principles of Islam that everything else must revolve around. Are there areas of your life where you are not passing through these poles—where your actions are cutting at oblique angles? What is one such area, and what concrete step will you take this week to reorient it so that it meets the standard at a right angle?

The Latitude of the Region of Visibility
Explains the concept of 'latitude of the region of visibility' (ʿarḍ iqlīm al-ruʾya).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the latitude of the region of visibility means and how it is identified as the smaller arc between specific celestial poles and horizons.

Core Idea
The latitude of visibility is always the smaller arc between pole and horizon.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif clarifies the technical term 'latitude of the region of visibility' introduced in defining the fourth celestial circle. The commentator explains when an arc exists and when it does not, and crucially, which arc is designated as the latitude. Understanding this concept is key to grasping the geometry of visibility conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: if the pole lies on the horizon, no arc exists; if separation occurs, an arc is conceived.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two cases: one involving the pole of the zodiac and the horizon, the other involving the pole of the horizon and the zodiac. Understand why an arc is absent when points coincide.
3
Focus on the definition of latitude
The passage defines the latitude as the smaller arc from the circle of azimuth. Compare the two arcs and note that the shorter one is called the 'latitude of the region of visibility'.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( بين الأفق وقطب منطقة البروج أي إذا وقع قطب المنطقة على جزء من الأفق في جانب الجنوب مثلاً . لم يتصور هناك قوس بين الأفق وبين قطب المنطقة إذ المفروض أنه لم يقع بينهما المفارقة ،بعد ، ثم إذا فارق قطب المنطقة بالحركة اليومية عن ذلك الجزء من الأفق يتصور القوس بين الأفق وبين قطب المنطقة وكذا إذا كان قطب أفق من الآفاق واقعاً على جزء من البروج لم يتصور هناك قوس بين ذلك الأفق وبين منطقة البروج وهو ظاهر. أما إذا كان قطب أفق الآفاق واقعاً على جزء من سطح الفلك غير جزء منطقة البروج فيتصور هناك قوس بين قطب الأفق ومنطقة البروج إذا عرفت هذا فالقوس الأصغر الواقع من دائرة السمت من قطب المنطقة وبين الجزء الأقرب إليه من أجزاء الأفق يسمى عرض إقليم الرؤية دون القوس الأطول منها بين الجزء الأبعد وبين ذلك القطب وكذا القوس الواقع منها بين قطب الأفق وبين الجزء الأقرب إليه من أجزاء المنطقة عرض إقليم الرؤية أيضاً كما ذكره فتأمل .
His saying: '(between the horizon and the pole of the zodiac) i.e., if the pole of the zodiac falls on a part of the horizon, in the south side for example. There is no arc conceived between the horizon and the pole of the zodiac because it is assumed that no separation has occurred between them. Then when the pole of the zodiac separates by the daily motion from that part of the horizon, an arc is conceived between the horizon and the pole of the zodiac. Similarly, if the pole of one of the horizons falls on a part of the zodiac, no arc is conceived between that horizon and the zodiac—this is clear. But if the pole of the horizon of horizons falls on a part of the surface of the sphere other than a part of the zodiac, then an arc is conceived between the pole of the horizon and the zodiac. If you understand this, then the smaller arc from the circle of azimuth between the pole of the zodiac and the nearest part of the horizon is called the latitude of the region of visibility (ʿarḍ iqlīm al-ruʾya), not the longer arc between the farther part and that pole. Likewise, the arc from it between the pole of the horizon and the nearest part of the zodiac is also called the latitude of the region of visibility, as he mentioned. So ponder.'
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage explains the term 'latitude of the region of visibility' (ʿarḍ iqlīm al-ruʾya) introduced in the definition of the fourth circle. The commentator clarifies by considering when the pole of the zodiac lies exactly on the horizon: in that case no arc exists because there is no separation. Only when the pole moves away from the horizon (by daily motion) does an arc appear. Similarly, if a horizon pole lies on the zodiac, no arc exists, but when it is elsewhere, an arc appears. The key point is that the latitude of visibility is always the smaller arc—either from the zodiac pole to the nearest horizon point, or from the horizon pole to the nearest zodiac point. The passage concludes by inviting reflection.

Key Terms
عرض إقليم الرؤية
قطب منطقة البروج
قطب الأفق
دائرة السمت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the latitude of visibility is any arc between pole and horizon.
Correction: The text specifies that it must be the smaller arc, not the longer one.

Checkpoint
When is there no arc of latitude of visibility between the pole of the zodiac and the horizon?
A) When the pole lies exactly on the horizon
B) When the pole is at the zenith
C) When the pole is at the nadir
D) Always when the pole is visible

Key Takeaway
The latitude of the region of visibility is defined as the smaller arc from the circle of azimuth between the pole of the zodiac and the nearest horizon point, or between the pole of the horizon and the nearest zodiac point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage ends with 'فَتَأَمَّل' — so ponder. When was the last time you truly gazed at the sky and reflected on the precision of Allah's creation? What is one specific step you can take today to cultivate this habit?
?
You have learned that the latitude of visibility is the shorter arc between two points. In your spiritual journey, are you taking the direct, shorter path towards Allah through obedience and reflection, or are you wandering in longer arcs of heedlessness? What is one 'shorter arc' you can commit to starting tomorrow?

The Circle of Altitude: Definition and Properties
Defines the fifth circle and its properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the circle of altitude, its relation to altitude and declination arcs, azimuth, and its coincidences with the meridian and the first heaven circle.

Core Idea
The circle of altitude reveals a celestial body's height and its azimuth direction.

Sanusi now explains the fifth of the astronomical circles: the circle of altitude. This circle is fundamental for understanding how we measure the height of stars and planets in the sky. In this passage, he defines the circle, distinguishes between altitude and declination arcs, introduces the concept of azimuth, and describes special cases when the circle coincides with the meridian or the first heaven circle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms: دائرة الارتفاع, دائرة الانحطاط, سمت.
2
Understand the definition and the two arcs
Identify how the circle is defined and what the altitude and declination arcs represent. Note that altitude is above the horizon, declination below.
The text clarifies that declination can be on either east or west side below the horizon.
3
Grasp the notion of azimuth
Azimuth (سمت) is the arc on the horizon between the circle of altitude and the east/west point. Notice when it does not exist.
When the altitude circle coincides with the first heaven circle, the planet has no azimuth.
4
Follow the coincidences with meridian and first heaven
The circle of altitude coincides with the meridian at maximum altitude and maximum declination (twice per day). Also, if the planet is on the first heaven circle, the altitude circle coincides with it.
At the zenith or nadir, it can coincide with both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والخامسة : ) منها ( تمر بقطبي الأفق وبكوكب ما أي وبرأس خط خارج من مركز العالم إلى سطح الفلك ماراً بمركزه ) وتسمى دائرة الارتفاع ) والانحطاط ( إذ قوس منها ) واقعة (بين الأفق وبين الكوكب من جانب المشرق ،ارتفاعه من جانب المغرب انحطاطه) والصواب أن القوس الأولى ارتفاعه الشرقي، والثانية ارتفاعه الغربي وأما الانحطاط فهو قوس منها تحت الأفق إما في جانب الغرب أو الشرق والقوس الواقعة من الأفق بين مع دائرة الارتفاع وبين إحدى نقطتي الشرق والغرب تسمى بالسمت فإذا انطقبت دائرة ارتفاع الكوكب على دائرة أول السماوات لم تكن له قوس سمت لمرورها حينئذ بنقطتي المشرق والمغرب ( وهذه الدائرة عند غاية ارتفاع الكوكب تنطبق بدائرة وسط السماء ) أعني نصف النهار، وكذا الحال عند غاية انحطاطه ففي كل دورة بالحركة الأولى تنطبق دائرة الارتفاع على نصف النهار مرتين وانطباقها عليها إنما يكون إن لم يكن الكوكب ) على دائرة أول السماوات و) تنطبق هذه الدائرة ( عليها ) أي على أول السماوات ( إن كان الكوكب (عليها ) وحينئذ لم يكن للكوكب سمت كما عرفت وهذا الانطباق إنما يظهر إذا لم يكن الكوكب في احدى الغايتين. وأما إذا فرض أنه في إحداهما مع كونه على دائرة أول السماوات كما إذا كان على سمت الرأس أو القدم. فإنه يجوز اعتبار انطباقها على كل واحدة من نصف النهار وأول السماوات
And the fifth: (of them) passes through the poles of the horizon and through some planet, i.e., through the head of a line extending from the center of the world to the surface of the sphere passing through its center. And it is called the circle of altitude (irtifāʿ) and declination (inḥiṭāṭ), since an arc from it lying between the horizon and the planet on the eastern side is its altitude, and on the western side its declination. The correct view is that the first arc is its eastern altitude, and the second its western altitude. As for declination, it is an arc from it below the horizon either in the west or east. And the arc from the horizon between the circle of altitude and one of the two points of east and west is called azimuth (samt). So if the circle of altitude of a planet coincides with the first heaven circle, it has no azimuth arc because it then passes through the east and west points. And this circle, at the planet's maximum altitude, coincides with the circle of the middle of the heaven, i.e., the meridian; similarly at maximum declination. Thus in each revolution by the first motion, the circle of altitude coincides with the meridian twice. Its coincidence with it occurs only if the planet is not on the first heaven circle. And this circle coincides with it (i.e., the first heaven) if the planet is on it. In that case the planet has no azimuth, as you know. This coincidence becomes apparent only if the planet is not at either of the two limits. But if it is assumed to be at one of them while being on the first heaven circle—as when it is at the zenith or nadir—then it is permissible to consider it as coinciding with both the meridian and the first heaven circle.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth circle, the circle of altitude (irtifāʿ) and declination (inḥiṭāṭ). It first states that it passes through the horizon's poles and a given planet, along a line from the Earth's center to the sphere's surface. It then explains that the arc above the horizon on the east is the planet's altitude, on the west its altitude (western), and the arc below the horizon is declination. The arc on the horizon between this circle and the east/west point is called azimuth. If the altitude circle coincides with the first heaven circle, the planet has no azimuth because it passes through east and west. The circle of altitude coincides with the meridian at the planet's maximum altitude and again at maximum declination—twice per day by the first motion—but only if the planet is not on the first heaven circle. If the planet is on the first heaven circle, the altitude circle coincides with it, and then the planet has no azimuth. However, at the extremes (zenith or nadir) on the first heaven, the altitude circle can be considered coinciding with both meridian and first heaven. The passage thus gives a complete description of the circle's definition and its various coincidences.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Declination (انحطاط) only occurs on the western side.
Correction: The text clarifies that declination can be on the west or east: 'تحت الأفق إما في جانب الغرب أو الشرق' (below the horizon either in the west or east).

Checkpoint
When does a planet have no azimuth?

B) When it is at the zenith
C) When it is at the meridian
D) When it is at the horizon

Key Takeaway
The circle of altitude is defined by a planet's position relative to the horizon, and its coincidences with the meridian and first heaven circle determine when azimuth exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In this passage, the circle of altitude is defined by its relationship to the horizon and the planet. Reflect on your own life: what is your 'horizon'—the framework through which you see the world? Is it aligned with the divine horizon of the Qur'an and Sunnah? What would it mean for your 'circle of altitude' to have no azimuth—to be perfectly aligned with the heavenly circles? Are you actively seeking that alignment, or are you content with a directionless path?
?
The circle of altitude coincides with the meridian at the moments of maximum altitude and maximum declination. How does your circle align with the straight path (sirat al-mustaqim) at those moments? Coincidence of the Altitude Circle with the Meridian and First Heaven
Explains conditions for altitude circle coincidence with meridian and first heaven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific conditions under which the altitude circle coincides with the meridian and the first heaven, and why the circle of azimuth is not considered in this context.

Core Idea
At zenith or nadir, the altitude circle aligns with both the meridian and the first heaven.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif delves into a nuanced astronomical point: the coincidence of the altitude circle with two major celestial circles at specific planetary positions. The author explains why this occurs and also clarifies why another circle, the azimuth, is excluded from consideration. Understanding these conditions deepens one's grasp of the celestial coordinate system as used in traditional Islamic cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument, then read the literal translation carefully.
The passage is a commentary, so it follows a logical sequence of justifications.
2
Identify the two coincidences
Note the two circles discussed: the first heaven and the meridian. The passage justifies each coincidence separately.
3
Understand the special case
The passage explains that at the zenith or nadir, the altitude circle coincides with both circles simultaneously, regardless of the planet's motion between them.
4
Consider the azimuth circle
The passage adds that at the zenith/nadir, the altitude circle also coincides with the circle of azimuth, but this is not taken into account because the azimuth circle is not fixed in any locality.
Contrast with the meridian and first heaven which are fixed.
5
Reflect on the final note
The last part comments on a phrase about circles not being multiple in one locality, clarifying that multiplicity refers to individuals of that locality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإنه يجوز اعتبار انطباقها على كل واحدة من نصف النهار ، وأول السماوات أما انطباقها على أول السماوات فظاهر إذ المفروض أن الكوكب يكون متحركاً على أول السماوات وأما انطباقها على نصف النهار فلأنه يصدق على نصف النهار أيضاً أنها دائرة تمر بقطبي الأفق وبمركز ذلك الكوكب. ولا اعتبار بحركة الكوكب على أول السماوات حتى لو فرضنا أن الكوكب قد تحرك فيما بين دائرتين نصف النهار وأول السماوات ثم وصل إلى سمت الرأس أو القدم لزم انطباق دائرة الارتفاع على الدائرتي المذكورتين معا مع أن حركة الكوكب هناك لم تكن على إحداهما. ثم لا يذهب عليك أن هذه الدائرة عند وصول الكوكب إلى سمت الرأس أو القدم تكون أيضاً منطبقة على دائرة السمت . إلا أنه لم يعتبر انطباقها عليها لأن دائرة السمت لم تكن متقررة في بقعة ما أصلاً بخلاف دائرتي نصف النهار وأول السماوات، فتأمل . قوله : (لا) تكون في بقعة واحدة متعددة وأما تعددهما باعتبار تعدد أشخاص تلك البقعة
His saying: '(Then it is permissible to consider it as coinciding with each of the meridian and the first heaven.) As for its coincidence with the first heaven, it is obvious, because it is assumed that the planet is moving on the first heaven. As for its coincidence with the meridian, because it holds true of the meridian as well that it is a circle passing through the poles of the horizon and through the center of that planet. And no consideration is given to the planet's motion on the first heaven; even if we assume that the planet has moved between the two circles of the meridian and the first heaven and then reached the zenith or nadir, the circle of altitude necessarily coincides with both mentioned circles, even though the planet's motion there was not on either of them. Moreover, do not overlook that this circle, when the planet reaches the zenith or nadir, also coincides with the circle of azimuth. However, its coincidence with it is not considered because the circle of azimuth is not established in any locality at all, unlike the two circles of the meridian and the first heaven. So ponder. His saying: 'not multiple in one locality'—and their multiplicity is in view of the multiplicity of individuals of that locality.'
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (qāl) on the previous matn statement that at the zenith or nadir on the first heaven, the altitude circle can be considered coinciding with both the meridian and the first heaven. The commentary justifies this: coincidence with the first heaven is obvious because the planet is on it; coincidence with the meridian is also justified because the meridian also passes through the horizon's poles and the planet's center. Even if the planet moved between these circles and reached the zenith, the altitude circle must coincide with both. Additionally, the circle of altitude also coincides with the circle of azimuth at the zenith/nadir, but this is not taken into account because the circle of azimuth is not fixed in any locality, unlike the meridian and first heaven which are fixed. The commentary ends with a note on the matn phrase about circles being 'not multiple in one locality', explaining that multiplicity refers to the individuals of that locality. This adds depth to understanding the coincidence conditions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The altitude circle always coincides with the meridian and first heaven.
Correction: It only coincides when the planet is at the zenith or nadir, not in general.

Checkpoint

D) Because the altitude circle is not defined there

Key Takeaway
The altitude circle at zenith or nadir coincides with both the meridian and the first heaven, but not with the azimuth circle due to its lack of fixed location in any locality.

?

Definitions of Concentric and Eccentric Spheres
The author introduces two benefits, then defines the concentric and eccentric spheres and the inclined sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the concentric sphere and the eccentric sphere, and how they relate to the inclined sphere and the simulative spheres in the Ptolemaic model.

Core Idea
The heavens are not all concentric; eccentrics and inclined spheres explain planetary motions.

The author prepares us for understanding the apparent irregularities of planetary motion by introducing two key geometrical devices: the concentric and eccentric spheres. These models underlie the apparent variations in speed, direction, and visibility. Before we can grasp the complex motions of the planets, we must first understand these fundamental celestial spheres.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two types of spheres
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that the author introduces two benefits, but only the first benefit is given in this passage.
2
Understand the concentric sphere
The concentric sphere has its center at the center of the earth/world. It has two surfaces: the convex (outer) and concave (inner). Make sure you can locate these terms in the Arabic.
The term 'muwāfiq al-markaz' literally means 'coinciding with the center'.
3
Understand the eccentric sphere and its nesting
The eccentric sphere surrounds the earth but its center is offset. It is placed within another sphere (the inclined or simulative sphere). For Mercury, there are two eccentrics nested. Trace this classification carefully.
Note that the inclined sphere is a concentric sphere that contains the eccentric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنختم هذا البحث بفائدتين تنفعانك فيما سيأتيك بعد من اختلاف حركات السيارات في الرؤية [عة؟] وبطئاً واستقامة ورجوعاً إذ لا بد لهذا الاختلاف من أصل يستند إليه. الأولى: الفلك الموافق المركز ما مركزه مركز العالم وهو مركز الأرض ويكون له أي للموافق المركز سطحان محيطان به من داخل وخارج هما محدبه وهو المحيط به من خارج ومقعره وهو الذي يقابله. والفلك الخارج المركز فلك محيط بالأرض ليس مركزه مركزها بل يقع أي يميل مركزه إلى جانب منها أي من مركز الأرض. ويكون الفلك الخارج المركز في ثخن فلك آخر ويسمى ذلك الفلك الآخر المائل. هذا إنما يصح في خارج القمر فإنه في ثخن فلك موافق المركز مسمى بالمائل. وما عداه السيارات سوى عطارد خوارجها في ثخن أفلاك موافقة المراكز مسماة بالممثلات. وأما عطارد فله خارجان أحدهما في ثخن الممثل والآخر في ثخن الخارج الأول كما ستعرفه.
And we will conclude this investigation with two benefits that will help you in what will come after regarding the difference in the motions of the planets in [؟] and slowness, directness and retrograde, for this difference must have a principle to rely on. First: The concentric sphere: its center is the center of the world, and it is the center of the earth, and it has two surfaces surrounding it from inside and outside: its convex (muhaddab) and its concave (muqaʿʿar). And the eccentric sphere: a sphere surrounding the earth, its center is not its center, but it falls — i.e., its center leans to a side of it — i.e., from the center of the earth. And the eccentric sphere is in the thickness of another sphere, and that other sphere is called the inclined sphere (al-māʾil). This is valid only for what is beyond the moon, for it is in the thickness of a concentric sphere called the inclined sphere, and apart from that, the planets except Mercury: their eccentrics are in the thickness of concentric spheres called the simulative spheres (al-mumaththalāt). And as for Mercury, it has two eccentrics: one in the thickness of the simulative sphere and the other in the thickness of the first eccentric, as you will know.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage regarding celestial spheres. It begins by stating that two benefits will be given to help understand planetary motions. The first benefit defines two types of spheres: the concentric sphere (al-falak al-muwāfiq al-markaz) and the eccentric sphere (al-falak al-khārij al-markaz). The concentric sphere has its center at the earth's center (the center of the world) and has two surfaces: the convex (muhaddab) outer surface and the concave (muqaʿʿar) inner surface. The eccentric sphere surrounds the earth but its center is offset from the earth's center. This eccentric sphere is located within another sphere, which is either the inclined sphere (al-māʾil) or, for planets other than Mercury, the simulative spheres (al-mumaththalāt). For Mercury, there are two eccentrics nested within these. The author thus establishes the fundamental geometrical model for the heavens.

Key Terms
الفلك الموافق المركز
الفلك الخارج المركز
المائل
الممثلات
المحدب
المقعر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All celestial spheres are concentric around the Earth.
Correction: The author clarifies that eccentric spheres exist, which have their centers offset from Earth's center, and these are nested within other spheres.

Checkpoint
What is the center of the concentric sphere?
A) The center of the earth
B) The center of the eccentric sphere
C) The center of the inclined sphere
D) The center of the universe

Key Takeaway
The concentric sphere shares Earth's center, while the eccentric sphere's center is offset, and these are nested within other spheres to model planetary motions.

The Two Complements of the Eccentric Sphere
Classification of the two complements with tapering thickness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eccentric sphere is divided into two complements—the encompassing and the encompassed—and how their thickness tapers from a maximum to a point.

Core Idea
The complements are two interlocking parts that, together with the eccentric, form a complete sphere.

In this passage, the author introduces the structure of an eccentric sphere by showing how the remaining space around the eccentric is split into two complementary parts. These parts, called complements, have thickness that gradually decreases until they touch the eccentric at a single point. This lesson focuses on that division and the tapering property.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the division
Read the first part of the Arabic passage, from 'وينقسم ذلك الفلك الآخر' to 'كما أن غلظ كل منهما ودقته تساوي غلظ الآخر ودقته'. Follow along with the translation to understand the two complements.
Notice the terms 'المتمم الحاوي' and 'المتمم المحوى'.
2
Identify the two complements
The passage divides the containing sphere into an encompassing complement (outside the eccentric) and an encompassed complement (inside the eccentric). Note their positions and how they meet at the tangent point.
3
Understand the tapering thickness
The complements are not uniform; they start thick at one end and taper to a thin point. Understand that this tapering is related to the eccentricity of the center.
The text says they 'increase from a thickness equal to the amount of the eccentricity' and then decrease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينقسم ذلك الفلك الآخر بواسطة كون الخارج في ثخنه إلى قسمين: أحدهما حاو للخارج والآخر محوله ويسميان بالمتممين إذ بانضمامهما إلى الخارج يتم الفلك الكلي الذي ذلك الخارج جزء منه هما ليسا متساويين في الثخن بل هما آخذان من غلظ هو بقدر خروج مركزه عن مركز العالم يتدرج ذلك الغلظ إلى دقة أي ينتقص شيئاً فشيئاً ويدق حتى ينتهي بنقطة مماسة للخارج المركز من أحدهما وهو المتمم الحاوي لمحدبه أي محدب الخارج ومن الآخر وهو المتمم المحوى لمقعره أي مقعر الخارج متبادلين حال من المستتر في آخذان أي هما يأخذان في ذلك الغلظ المتدرج المنتهي إلى ما ذكر حال كونهما متبادلين في الغلظ والدقة فيكون غلظ كل من المتممين في مقابلة الدقة من الآخر بحيث يكون حجم مجموع المحوى الداخل في الخارج والحاوي الخارج عنه معاً في جميع الأجزاء سواء لأن دقة أحدهما تنجبر بغلظ الآخر ويكون في الوسط منهما أي من المتممين حجمهما سواء أي يكون حجم وسط كل منهما مساوياً لحجم وسط الآخر كما أن غلظ كل منهما ودقته تساوي غلظ الآخر ودقته. ( ويكون مقعر الداخلين المحوى موازياً لمحدب الخارجين الحاوي (و) يكون مركزهما أي مركز المقعر والمحدب المتوازيين ( واحداً) هو مركز العالم وهذا إنما يصح إذا كان الخارج في ثخن فلك موافق المركز وأما إذا كان في ثخن خارج آخر كأحد خارجي عطارد، فإن مركز السطحين المتوازيين يكون حينئذ مركز الخارج الآخر، وهذه الأحكام المتعلقة بالمتممين كلها صحيحة سوى الحكم بأن غلظ كل منهما يساوي مقدار خروج المركز إذ الصواب أن غلظ كل منهما ضعف ذلك المقدار كما قام عليه البرهان ويشهد له أيضاً التخيل الصحيح ممن له أدنى مسكة. قوله: ( إذ الصواب إلخ) هذا إنما يرد لو كان المراد من الحيز ما يخرج أما إذا كان بمعناه المصدري، وتكون المعنى بمقدار يحصل بخروج مركزه مركز العالم وهو ضعف ما بين المركزين فلا كما لا يخفى. قوله: (سوى الحكم بأن غلظ كل منها إلخ ) يمكن أن يقال في تصحيح كلام المصنف إن معنى قوله: بقدر خروج المركز أي بحسب خروج المركز، وإن أحدهما ناظر إلى الآخر لا على معنى أن غلظ كل من المتممين يساوي مقدار خروج المركز حتى يرد عليه اعتراض الشارح. وبالجملة إن غلظ المتمم يكون ناظراً إلى خروج المركز، فإن كان ذلك المقدار مثلاً كان غلظ المتمم مثلى قطر الأرض. وهكذا يكون غلظ المتمم ضعف مقدار خروج المركز يظهر ذلك الأشكال المرسومة في الإدراك لتصوير الأفلاك الخارجة المركز فارجع إليها وسيجيء زيادة بيان إن شاء الله تعالى.
And that other sphere is divided, by virtue of the eccentric being in its thickness, into two parts: one encompassing the eccentric and the other being encompassed, and they are called the complements (al-mumimman), since by their conjunction with the eccentric the complete sphere is formed, of which that eccentric is a part. They are not equal in thickness, but rather they increase from a thickness equal to the amount of the eccentricity of its center from the center of the world, and that thickness gradually decreases to thinness, i.e., it decreases little by little and becomes thin until it ends at a point tangent to the eccentric from one of them, which is the encompassing complement at its convex surface (muhaddab), i.e., the convex of the eccentric; and from the other, which is the encompassed complement at its concave (muqaʿʿar), i.e., the concave of the eccentric, alternating — a state of the understood in 'they increase' — i.e., they take on that gradual thickness ending to what was mentioned while alternating in thickness and thinness. So the thickness of each of the complements is opposite the thinness of the other, such that the volume of the total of the encompassed (the inner) within the eccentric and the encompassing (the outer) around it together are equal in all parts, because the thinness of one is compensated by the thickness of the other. And in the middle of them, i.e., of the complements, their volumes are equal, i.e., the volume of the middle of each equals the volume of the middle of the other, just as the thickness and thinness of each equal the thickness and thinness of the other. And the concave of the inner (encompassed) is parallel to the convex of the outer (encompassing), and their center, i.e., the center of the parallel concave and convex, is one, which is the center of the world. This is valid only when the eccentric is in the thickness of a concentric sphere. But if it is in the thickness of another eccentric, like one of Mercury's two eccentrics, then the center of the two parallel surfaces is the center of the other eccentric. And all these rulings concerning the complements are correct except the ruling that the thickness of each equals the amount of the eccentricity; rather the correct view is that the thickness of each is double that amount, as has been established by proof and attested by correct visualization for anyone with minimal understanding. His saying: (since the correct etc.) This can only be objected if by 'amount' is meant the distance of the eccentricity; but if it is meant in a causative sense, then the meaning is 'by an amount that results from the eccentricity of its center from the center of the world', which is double the distance between the two centers, so it is not obscure. His saying: (except the ruling that the thickness of each etc.) It can be said in correction of the author's statement that his saying 'by the amount of the eccentricity' means 'according to the eccentricity', and that one is relative to the other, not meaning that the thickness of each complement equals the amount of the eccentricity such that the commentator's objection arises. In short, the thickness of the complement is relative to the eccentricity; for if that amount were such, then the thickness of the complement would be twice the diameter of the earth. Thus the thickness of the complement is double the amount of the eccentricity, as is shown in the figures drawn for depicting the eccentric spheres, so refer to them, and further explanation will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a classification with explanation and a correction. It first divides the containing sphere into two complements (mumimman) when an eccentric sphere lies within its thickness. The complements are one encompassing and one encompassed. Their thicknesses are not equal but taper gradually from a maximum (equal to the eccentricity) to a minimum at the tangent point. They alternate in thickness and thinness such that the volume of the entire sphere is uniform, because the thinness of one part is offset by the thickness of the other. The center of the parallel concave and convex surfaces is the center of the world when the eccentric is in a concentric sphere. The author then corrects a common error: the thickness of each complement is actually double the eccentricity, not equal to it. The commentator then explains this correction: the phrase 'by the amount of the eccentricity' can be understood in a causative sense, meaning the thickness results from the eccentricity, and the correct relation is double.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two complements have equal thickness throughout.
Correction: The thickness tapers; they are not equal in thickness but vary, with a maximum at one end and a minimum at the tangent point.

Checkpoint
What are the two complements called?
A) The encompassing (al-ḥāwī) and the encompassed (al-muḥawā)
B) The inner and the outer
C) The thick and the thin
D) The eccentric and the concentric

Key Takeaway
The eccentric sphere is surrounded by two complementary parts that taper in thickness and together with the eccentric form a complete sphere.

Equal Volume Condition and Thickness Correction
Condition for parallel surfaces and correction of thickness relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition that the concave and convex surfaces are parallel with a common center, and the correction that the thickness of each complement is double the eccentricity, not equal to it.

Core Idea
The thinness of one complement is compensated by the thickness of the other, ensuring uniform total volume.

The author continues by explaining a geometric condition for the complements: their concave and convex surfaces must be parallel, sharing a common center. He then corrects a common mistake regarding the thickness of each complement relative to the eccentricity, stating that it is actually double, as established by proof and visualization. This lesson unpacks that condition and the correction.

Guided Path
1
Read the condition about parallel surfaces
Read from 'ويكون مقعر الداخلين المحوى' to 'وهذه الأحكام المتعلقة بالمتممين كلها صحيحة'. Understand the condition that the concave of the inner complement is parallel to the convex of the outer complement, and their center is the center of the world.
This only holds when the eccentric lies in a concentric sphere; otherwise, the center shifts.
2
Examine the correction of thickness
Read from 'سوى الحكم بأن غلظ كل منهما' to the end. The author corrects that the thickness of each complement is double the eccentricity, not equal. Note the commentator's explanation clarifying the phrase 'by the amount of the eccentricity'.
3
Reflect on the proof and visualization
Consider why the thickness must be double. The author says proof and correct visualization support this. Think about the geometry: the eccentricity is the offset distance, so the maximum thickness on each side adds up to twice that offset.
You can imagine a diagram to confirm.

The Epicycle (al-tadwīr): Definition and Motion
The author defines the epicycle and describes its motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and motion of the epicycle in Ptolemaic astronomy.

Core Idea
The epicycle is a solid sphere embedded in a carrier, tracing a circle as its center moves.

In this passage, the author defines the epicycle—a key component of Ptolemaic astronomy—as a solid sphere embedded within another sphere (the carrier). He explains its dimensions, its motion, and how it traces a circle that is concentric or eccentric depending on the carrier. This lesson unpacks that definition and clarifies a potential confusion about carriers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's description of the epicycle.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'tadwīr', 'ḥāmil', 'muwāfiq', and 'khārij'.
2
Understand the epicycle's definition
The epicycle is a solid sphere (not hollow) embedded in the thickness of another sphere. Its convex touches the carrier at one point and its concave at another, and its diameter equals the carrier's thickness.
The epicycle has no concave because it is solid; we assume it as a solid sphere.
3
Trace its motion
The center of the epicycle moves with the carrier sphere, tracing a circle. If the carrier is concentric, the traced circle is concentric; if the carrier is eccentric, the traced circle is also eccentric.
This motion explains planetary motion in Ptolemaic models.
4
Resolve the carrier objection
The commentator addresses that the carrier can be the inclined sphere, simulative spheres, or eccentrics. He argues that the discussion holds even if carriers are only eccentric, but also notes an exception like the sun.
This clarifies that the definition is general.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why understanding this definition and motion is foundational for later discussions on planetary orbits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتدوير عبارة عن كرة سوى الكوكب غير شاملة للأرض بل مركوزة في ثخن فلك بحيث يماس محدبه بنقطة ومقعره بأخرى وحينئذ يكون قطره بقدر ثخن ذلك الفلك ولا يتصور له أي للتدوير مقعر إذ لا حاجة بنا إلى مقعره فيفرض أنه كرة مصمتة ويتحرك مركزه بحركة الفلك الذي هو في تخنه دائر أحوال مركز العالم ويرسم التدوير بمركزه المتحرك بتلك الحركة دائرة مركزها مركز الفلك الحامل للتدوير إن كان الحامل موافقاً في المركز لمركز العالم كانت تلك الدائرة كذلك وإن كان الحامل خارجاً كانت الدائرة أيضاً خارجة المركز. قوله: ( وإن كان الحامل إلخ) اعلم أن حامل التدوير يكون شاملاً للمائل وسائر المتمثلات والخوارج المركز فلا يرد ما يقال: إنه لا يكون الحامل إلا ما هو خارج المركز فيلزم أن يكون قوله: إن كان الحامل موافقاً في المركز مجرد تقدير لم يكن مطابقاً لما هو في نفس الأمر إذ ليس لنا في نفس الأمر حامل يكون موافقاً في المركز. ولك أن تقول أيضا: إنه يمكن أن الكلام على القول بأن الشمس لم يكن لها خارج المركز بل كان لها فلك التدوير فقط فعلى هذا يكون حامل التدوير موافق المركز البتة على ما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى. قوله: (بل المتحرك وجه هذا الإضراب ظاهر، ثم إن المتحرك بحركة الكوكب يمكن أن يبني
And the epicycle (al-tadwīr) is a sphere other than the planet, not encompassing the earth, but embedded in the thickness of a sphere such that its convex touches at one point and its concave at another, and then its diameter equals the thickness of that sphere. It has no concave, i.e., the epicycle has no concave, because we have no need for its concave, so it is assumed to be a solid sphere. Its center moves with the motion of the sphere in whose thickness it is, rotating around the center of the world, and the epicycle traces with its moving center a circle whose center is the center of the carrier sphere (al-falak al-ḥāmil) of the epicycle. If the carrier is concentric (muwāfiq) in center with the center of the world, that circle is likewise concentric; and if the carrier is eccentric (khārij), the circle is also eccentric. His saying: (and if the carrier etc.) Know that the carrier of the epicycle encompasses the inclined sphere (al-māʾil) and all the simulative spheres (al-mumaththalāt) and the eccentrics, so it cannot be objected that the carrier is only what is eccentric, for then his statement 'if the carrier is concentric in center' would be mere supposition not corresponding to reality, since in reality we have no carrier that is concentric. But you may also say: It is possible that the discussion is based on the opinion that the sun has no eccentric but only an epicycle, so on this view the carrier of the epicycle is certainly concentric, as will come, God willing. His saying: (rather the moving) The reason for this apocope is clear. Then the moving (i.e., the planet) by the motion of the epicycle can be constructed...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the epicycle (tadwīr) and explains its motion. The epicycle is a solid sphere (not hollow) that does not enclose the earth. It is embedded in the thickness of another sphere (the carrier sphere) and touches it at two points. Its diameter equals the thickness of the carrier sphere. The epicycle has no concave surface because it is solid. Its center moves with the motion of the carrier sphere, and as it moves, it traces a circle. If the carrier sphere is concentric (its center is the center of the world), the traced circle is concentric; if the carrier is eccentric, the traced circle is also eccentric. The commentator then clarifies that the carrier of the epicycle can be either the inclined sphere, the simulative spheres, or eccentrics. He addresses a potential objection that only eccentric spheres can be carriers, explaining that the discussion might assume a scenario (like the sun) where the carrier is concentric. The fragment at the end suggests further elaboration on the motion of the planet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The epicycle is a hollow sphere like other celestial spheres.
Correction: The epicycle is a solid sphere (kura muṣmata), not hollow. It has no concave interior and its diameter equals the thickness of the carrier sphere.

Key Takeaway

Correction of the Thickness of Complementary Spheres
Correction of thickness relation in complementary spheres

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the thickness of complementary spheres is double the eccentricity, not equal, and how demonstration and textual interpretation support this correction.

Core Idea
The thickness of complementary spheres is twice the eccentricity, not equal.

In this passage, the commentator corrects a common mistake regarding the complementary spheres. While the author of the Mawāqif stated that thickness equals eccentricity, the commentator argues from both demonstration and geometry that it is actually double. This correction is essential for understanding the structure of the spheres and the precision of astronomical models.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main correction
Read the passage and locate the specific judgment being corrected. Note the phrase 'sawa al-hukm bi-anna ghulz kull minhuma yusawi miqdar kharuj al-markaz' and its correction.
The correction is: thickness equals double the eccentricity.
2
Understand the two interpretations of 'amount'
The commentator explains that the objection only applies if 'amount' (al-hayz) means the actual distance; if it means the verbal sense (the act of exiting), then the amount is double. Grasp this distinction.
This saves the author's wording from inconsistency.
3
Follow the geometric proof
The commentator provides a demonstration using points A, B, C on the convexity. Visualize how the volume of the sphere relates to the eccentricity.
Refer to the diagram mentioned as 'al-ashkal al-marsuma' for clarity.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
The commentator offers an alternative reading of the author's phrase: 'bi-qadr kharuj al-markaz' means 'according to' not 'equal to'. Understand how this reconciles the statement.
This shows the commentator's effort to harmonize with the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثالث : في فلك الثوابت الأحكام المتعلقة بالمتممين كلها صحيحة سوى الحكم بأن غلظ كل منهما يساوي مقدار خروج المركز إذ الصواب أن غلظ كل منهما ضعف ذلك المقدار كما قام عليه البرهان ويشهد له أيضاً التخيل الصحيح ممن له أدنى مسكة . قوله : ( إذ الصواب إلخ) هذا إنما يرد لو كان المراد من الحيز ما يخرج أما إذا كان بمعناه المصدري، وتكون المعنى بمقدار يحصل بخروج مركزه مركز العالم وهو ضعف ما بين المركزين فلا كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( كما قام عليه (البرهان بيانه أنا إذا فرضنا أن ا ب ج محدب فلك يكون الخارج في تحته وده ومقعره فمن وإلى أدا أو من إلى ب ومن ذ إلى ج يكون حجم ذلك الفلك . قوله : (سوى الحكم بأن غلظ كل منها إلخ ) يمكن أن يقال في تصحيح كلام المصنف إن معنى قوله : بقدر خروج المركز أي بحسب خروج المركز، وإن أحدهما ناظر إلى الآخر لا على معنى أن غلظ كل من المتممين يساوي مقدار خروج المركز حتى يرد عليه اعتراض الشارح . وبالجملة إن غلظ المتمم يكون ناظراً إلى خروج المركز ، فإن كان ذلك المقدار مثلاً كان غلظ المتمم مثلى قطر الأرض. وهكذا يكون غلظ المتمم ضعف مقدار خروج المركز يظهر ذلك الأشكال المرسومة في الإدراك لتصوير الأفلاك الخارجة المركز فارجع إليها وسيجيء زيادة بيان إن شاء الله تعالى .
The First Observation - Second Chapter - First Section - Third Goal: On the Sphere of the Fixed Stars. All the judgments related to the complementary spheres are correct except the judgment that the thickness of each of them equals the amount of the eccentricity. Indeed, the correct is that the thickness of each is double that amount, as proven by demonstration, and sound imagination bears witness to it for even the least intelligent. His saying: '(Indeed, the correct, etc.)' This only applies if by 'amount' is meant what comes out. But if it is meant in the verbal sense, then the meaning is an amount obtained by the eccentricity of its center from the center of the world, which is double the distance between the two centers, so it is not as is hidden. His saying: '(As proven by demonstration)' Its explanation: If we suppose that A B C is the convexity of a sphere, then the exterior in its interior and its concavity, and from and to A or from to B and from D to C, that is the volume of that sphere. His saying: '(Except the judgment that the thickness of each of them, etc.)' It can be said in correction of the author's statement that the meaning of his saying 'by the amount of the eccentricity' is 'according to the eccentricity,' and that one is related to the other, not that the thickness of each of the complements equals the amount of the eccentricity such that the commentator's objection applies. In summary, the thickness of the complement is related to the eccentricity; if that amount is, for example, the thickness of the complement is twice the diameter of the earth. Thus, the thickness of the complement is double the amount of the eccentricity. This appears in the figures drawn in perceptibles for depicting the eccentric spheres. Refer to them, and more explanation will come if God wills.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a correction of a judgment regarding the complementary spheres. The author had stated that the thickness of each complementary sphere equals the amount of eccentricity (kharuj al-markaz). The commentator argues that this is incorrect and that the correct thickness is double the eccentricity. The commentator first explains that the objection only arises if 'amount' (al-hayz) is interpreted as the actual distance; but if interpreted in the verbal sense as the act of exiting, then the amount obtained by the exit of the center from the center of the world is indeed double the distance between the two centers, so the thickness is double. He then provides a geometric demonstration: supposing points A, B, C on the convexity, the volume of the sphere can be calculated. Finally, he offers an alternative interpretation of the author's phrasing: 'by the amount of eccentricity' means 'according to the eccentricity,' not equality. The thickness is related to the eccentricity such that if the eccentricity is a certain amount, the thickness is twice the earth's diameter. He refers to figures for the eccentric spheres and promises further explanation.

Correction: The correct thickness is double the eccentricity, as proven by demonstration and supported by sound imagination.

The thickness of a complementary sphere is always double the eccentricity, as proven by geometry and sound reasoning.
