Second Intention: Lunar Eclipse
(On the lunar eclipse, and it is that it may be (the moon opposite the sun) near the two nodes, so the earth) then (lies between it and the sun, so the earth prevents its light from it, and it is seen dull as its original color, and because the body of the earth is much smaller than the body of the sun, so the shadow arising from the earth falls (conical), its base a small circle on the earth and its apex aligned with a part of the parts of the zodiac opposite to a part in which the sun is located. (If the moon at the time of opposition has no latitude, being at one of the nodes, it is completely eclipsed because it is smaller than the earth—indeed, smaller than the thickness of the shadow where it reaches it, so it falls entirely inside it and remains in it for a time. (And if it has latitude, if that latitude is (equal to half the radius of the disk of the moon
His saying: (where it reaches) i.e., it reaches it, i.e., to the moon, so it falls entirely, i.e., all of the moon, inside it, i.e., the shadow, and remains in it, i.e., inside it, by the amount of half the radius of the moon's disk, etc. Because the center of the shadow's circle is on the zodiac belt, and the center of the moon's disk is on the circumference of its inclined orbit, so half of each of the diameters of the moon's disk and the shadow circle lie between the two belts. In the case of equality of the moon's latitude to the sum of the two mentioned diameters, the moon's disk is tangent to the shadow circle from the outside at a point in the direction of latitude like the convexity of the two circles, so nothing of the shadow falls on the moon's disk. Similarly, if the moon's latitude is more than the sum of the two halves.
His saying: (its base) i.e., the base of the shadow arising from the earth. And his saying: a small circle, and this small circle is parallel to the great circle bisecting the earth's sphere but smaller than it, because the body of the sun is much larger than the body of the earth. So that great circle is not the origin of the shadow because sunlight falls on the circumference of that great circle as well. So how could it be the origin of the shadow with light surrounding it? Rather, the circle that is smaller than that circle, but this circle which is the origin of the shadow must be parallel to that great circle. Then it follows that the base of the cone is a small circle as well, as he mentioned. And his saying: aligned with a part, etc., means that in the zodiac, two opposite parts are considered: one is where the sun is, and the other is aligned with the apex of that cone.
His saying: equal to half the radius of the disk of the moon, and he did not consider the whole of each one of the moon and the cone's circle because what is considered here is the inclination of the moon's center from the center of the cone's circle. So if the moon's center deviates from the center of the cone's circle by the amount of half its diameter and half its diameter together, no barrier from the earth remains between it and the sun at all. And his saying: 'outside' the implicit pronoun in it refers to the surface of the moon's body, so it is either a state of it or a second object for the mentioned supposition, i.e., from imagining the surface of the moon's body as a round surface
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage explains the lunar eclipse. It begins with the definition: the moon opposite the sun near the nodes, with earth between, blocking sunlight. The earth's body is smaller than the sun's, so its shadow is conical, with a small circular base on earth and apex towards the zodiac. If the moon has no latitude (at a node), it is fully eclipsed as it enters the thicker shadow. If it has latitude equal to half the moon's disk radius, the disk just touches the shadow's edge externally, so no eclipse occurs. The gloss elaborates: the shadow cone's base is a small circle parallel to the earth's equator but smaller because the sun's larger size means the full shadow narrows. The condition for an eclipse is not just the moon's center within the shadow, but the full disk; if the moon's center is offset by half its radius plus half the shadow's radius, it escapes. The text ends mid-sentence discussing the surface of the moon imagined as round.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A lunar eclipse is caused by the moon passing between the earth and sun.
Correction: Actually, a lunar eclipse occurs when the earth is between the sun and moon, and the moon enters the earth's shadow.

Checkpoint
When does a total lunar eclipse occur according to the passage?
A) When the moon is at opposition without latitude.
B) When the moon has latitude equal to half its radius.
C) When the moon is directly between the sun and earth.
D) When the moon is full and far from the nodes.

Key Takeaway
The lunar eclipse is a natural phenomenon explained by geometry: the moon must be opposite the sun and near a node to enter the earth's shadow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conditions for Lunar Eclipse: No Eclipse, Partial, Total
Classifies types of lunar eclipse based on moon's position relative to Earth's shadow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four geometric conditions that determine whether a lunar eclipse occurs, and if so, whether it is partial or total with or without duration.

Core Idea
Lunar eclipse types depend on the moon's position relative to Earth's shadow cone.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif classifies lunar eclipses based on the moon's position relative to the Earth's shadow cone. It outlines four distinct geometric conditions that determine whether there is no eclipse, a partial eclipse, a total eclipse without duration, or a total eclipse with duration. Understanding these conditions is essential for grasping the astronomical reasoning in traditional Islamic cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Note the technical terms like 'shadow cone' and 'circle of the shadow'.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the four conditions
Look for the four cases described: no eclipse (touching from outside), partial eclipse (less than sum of radii), total eclipse with no duration (equal to excess), total eclipse with duration (less than excess).
The text uses conditional 'if' statements.
3
Understand the key geometric variables
Focus on the width (the distance between centers?), the sum of radii, and the excess of shadow radius over moon radius. These determine the type.
The commentary explains that 'excess' is the difference between the radius of the shadow and the radius of the moon.
4
Reflect on the glosses
The passage includes glosses clarifying definitions like 'circle of the shadow' and 'no duration'. Pay attention to these clarifications.
The glosses are marked by 'his statement'.

And the radius of the circle of the shadow, which is the circle occurring on the cone of the shadow from the imagination of the surface of the moon's body which is seen as a circle externally until the cone ends, the moon does not eclipse then, but rather it touches the shadow from outside like the humps of two circles. And if that width is less than the sum of the two mentioned radii, part of it eclipses, and that is to the extent of the intersection of the two diameters, meaning their meeting and interpenetration. If it is assumed that this lesser width equals the excess of the radius of the shadow over the radius of the moon, it eclipses entirely and its surface touches the circle of the shadow from inside and it has no duration. Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the types of lunar eclipse based on the moon's position relative to the Earth's shadow. It begins by describing the shadow cone and the circle of the shadow. Then it presents conditions: first, if the moon only touches the shadow from outside, it does not eclipse; second, if the width is less than the sum of the radii, a partial eclipse occurs proportional to the intersection; third, if the smaller width equals the excess of the shadow's radius over the moon's radius, a total eclipse occurs without any duration (the moon immediately emerges); fourth, if the width is even smaller, a total eclipse with duration occurs. The glosses clarify definitions: the circle of the shadow is a circle on the cone parallel to its base, and its center is on the ecliptic. The excess refers to the difference between the shadow's radius and the moon's radius. The text explains that 'no duration' means the moon is completely immersed but the shadow does not cover it for a long time. Finally, it clarifies that the expression 'part of it eclipses' is general and can also cover total eclipse.

Key Terms
خسوف
ظل
مخروط
مكث
فضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A total lunar eclipse always involves a period of darkness (duration).
Correction: The text shows that if the moon's distance equals the excess, the total eclipse has no duration—the moon immediately emerges from the shadow.

Checkpoint
What condition results in a total lunar eclipse without any duration?
A) The moon's width equals the sum of the radii of the moon and shadow.
B) The moon's width equals the excess of the shadow's radius over the moon's radius.
C) The moon's width is less than that excess.
D) The moon's width is greater than the sum.

Key Takeaway
The four geometric conditions for lunar eclipse are: no eclipse, partial eclipse, total without duration, and total with duration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between touching the shadow from outside and actually entering it. In your spiritual life, are there 'sins' that you only touch externally—perhaps out of ignorance or carelessness—but avoid full immersion? How does this geometric precision challenge you to examine the depth of your involvement with prohibited matters?
?
The condition for a total eclipse without duration requires the moon to be exactly tangent inside the shadow. This shows that even a hair's breadth difference changes the outcome. Reflect on your intentions: are there areas where you are 'exactly on the line' between obedience and disobedience? What would it take for you to ensure you are fully in the light?
?
If the slightest geometrical difference determines whether an eclipse has duration or not, what does that imply about how you use your time? Are you living with a sense of urgency, knowing that your opportunities for good deeds may have a 'duration' that could end suddenly? How can you make the most of the time you have been given?

Solar Eclipse: Definition and Conditions
Defines solar eclipse and explains its conditional variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a solar eclipse, why it occurs, and the conditions that determine its type (total, annular, or partial).

Core Idea
A solar eclipse is an apparent obscuration, not a change in the sun.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the astronomical basis of solar eclipses within a theological context. It clarifies that an eclipse is not a change in the sun's essence but a visual obstruction by the moon. Understanding this distinction is crucial to refute superstitious beliefs and align natural phenomena with divine order.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the passage carefully to see how the author defines solar eclipse as a visible meeting of the moon and sun, not a real one. Note the contrast with lunar eclipse.
The author stresses that the sun itself does not change.
2
Grasp the key conditions
Identify the conditions that determine the type of eclipse: the moon's latitude, its distance (perigee/apogee), and the sun's distance.
Pay attention to the rare annular eclipse scenario.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why the author emphasizes that the sun's essence remains unchanged. How does this counteract superstitious beliefs about eclipses?
This connects to the broader purpose of Ilm al-Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث: كسوف الشمس. في كسوف الشمس فنقول عند اجتماع القمر بالشمس في النهار اجتماعاً مرئياً لا حقيقياً إن لم يكن للقمر عرض مرئي حجب بيننا وبين الشمس لوقوعه على الخط الخارج من أبصارنا إليها فلم نر ضوء الشمس بل نرى لون القمر الكمد في وجه الشمس فنظن أن الشمس ذهب ضوؤها وهو الكسوف فليس الكسوف بغير حال في ذات الشمس كالخسوف في ذات القمر ولذلك أمكن أن يقع كسوف بالقياس إلى قوم دون قوم ويكون ذلك بقدر صفحة القمر فربما كسف الشمس كلها وإن كان أصغر منها وذلك لأنه أقرب إلينا في وتر قطره الزاوية التي توترها الشمس كمداً فتحجب به عنا بتمامها. وربما تكون الشمس وقت انكسافها في حضيضها فلقربها منا ترى أكبر ويكون القمر حينئذ في أوجه، فلبعده عنا يرى أصغر فلا يكسف جميع صفحتها بل تبقى منها حلقة نور محيطة به وقد روي أنها أي الحلقة النورانية رؤيت على وجهها في بعض الكسوفات مع ندرته وإن كان للقمر في ذلك الاجتماع عرض مرئي فإن كان ذلك العرض بقدر مجموع نصف قطرهما لم يكسفها وإن كان أكثر منهما فبالطريق الأولى وإن كان أقل منهما كسفها بقدر ذلك كما لا يخفى. قوله: (اجتماعاً مرئياً لا حقيقاً) وذلك لأن الشمس والقمر ليسا في فلك واحد حتى يجتمعان اجتماعاً حقيقياً. وقوله: يوترها الشمس أي يوترها قطر الشمس.
The third purpose: Solar eclipse. Regarding solar eclipse, we say: When the moon meets the sun during the day in a visible meeting, not a real one, if the moon has no visible latitude, it blocks between us and the sun because it lies on the line from our eyes to it, so we do not see the sun's light but we see the dull color of the moon in the face of the sun, so we think that the sun's light has gone, and that is the eclipse. So the eclipse is not a change in the sun itself like the lunar eclipse is in the moon itself, and therefore it is possible that an eclipse occurs relative to some people and not others. And that is to the extent of the disk of the moon, so it may eclipse the entire sun even though it (the moon) is smaller, because it is closer to us, and the angle of the chord subtended by the sun is covered, so it blocks it from us completely. And sometimes the sun at the time of its eclipse is in its perigee, so due to its proximity to us it appears larger, and the moon then is in its apogee, so due to its distance from us it appears smaller, so it does not eclipse its entire disk, but rather a ring of light remains surrounding it, and it has been narrated that that luminous ring was seen on its face in some eclipses, though rarely. And if the moon in that meeting has a visible latitude, then if that latitude equals the sum of their radii, it does not eclipse it; if it is greater, a fortiori it does not eclipse; and if it is less, it eclipses it to that extent, as is not hidden. His statement: (a visible meeting, not real) because the sun and moon are not in one sphere so they meet in a real meeting. And his statement: it subtends the sun means the diameter of the sun subtends it.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines solar eclipse and explains its conditions. It begins by contrasting solar eclipse with lunar: solar eclipse is not a change in the sun but an obscuration by the moon. It then describes that it occurs when the moon passes between us and the sun without visible latitude. It mentions that the moon can fully cover the sun even though smaller because it is closer. It then discusses the case when the sun is at perigee and moon at apogee, resulting in an annular eclipse (ring of light). Finally, it discusses the role of the moon's latitude: if equal to sum of radii, no eclipse; if greater, no eclipse; if less, partial eclipse. The glosses clarify that the meeting is only apparent, not real, and that 'subtends' refers to the sun's diameter.

Key Terms
كسوف
كمد
صفحة
حضيض
أوج
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A solar eclipse involves a change in the sun itself.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that a solar eclipse is not a change in the sun's essence but an obscuration by the moon.

Checkpoint
Why can the moon, despite being smaller than the sun, completely cover it during a solar eclipse?
A) Because the sun is farther away.
B) Because the moon is closer to Earth.
C) Because the moon's diameter is actually larger.
D) Because of atmospheric refraction.

Key Takeaway
A solar eclipse is not a change in the sun but an obscuration by the moon, and its type depends on the moon's latitude and the distances of the sun and moon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a solar eclipse, does your heart tremble as if something has changed in the sun itself? How can knowing that it is only an obstruction shift your awe from fear of change to wonder at the precision of Allah's creation?
?
The lesson shows that a solar eclipse may be visible to some but not others. Is there a truth in your life that you thought was absolute, only to realize it was based on your limited perspective? What step can you take today to seek a wider horizon?
?
The moon's distance from Earth determines whether it covers the sun entirely or leaves a ring of light. What 'rings of light'—blessings or signs—do you overlook because you are focused on what seems to block them? How can you train your sight to see both the obstacle and the light?

Ibn al-Haytham's Theory of Lunar Phases and Its Refutation
Presents Ibn al-Haytham's theory and refutes it using lunar eclipse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Haytham's theory that the moon's phases result from its own rotation, and why the occurrence of lunar eclipses refutes this theory.

Core Idea
If the moon's phases were due to its rotation, lunar eclipses would never occur.

The scholar Ibn al-Haytham proposed that the moon's changing phases could be explained if it were a sphere, half-illuminated, rotating on its axis. This lesson presents his theory and the powerful refutation using the phenomenon of lunar eclipse.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn al-Haytham's theory
Read the passage describing his proposal. Note that he suggests the moon is a luminous sphere with half illuminated, rotating so that different amounts of the illuminated half face us.
The theory is presented as a possibility (يجوز).
2
Identify the refutation
The passage states that this theory is refuted by lunar eclipse: if his theory were true, the moon would never be eclipsed. The commentary will explain why.
The refutation relies on the fact that during a lunar eclipse, the moon is opposite the sun, so if the illuminated half always faced the sun, it would always be full, never eclipsed.
3
Reflect on the argument
Consider how a single observed phenomenon (lunar eclipse) can disprove a theoretical model. This shows the importance of empirical evidence in Islamic theology and science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن ابن الهيثم قال في اختلاف تشكلات القمر: إنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف لأن القمر كرة مضيئة نصفها دون نصف وأنها أي تلك الكرة تدور على مركز نفسها بحركة مساوية لحركة فلكها فإذا كان نصفه المضيء إلينا كما في حال المقابلة فبدر أو المظلم كما في حال المقارنة فمحاق وفيما بينهما يختلف قدر ما نراه من المضيء هلالياً ونصف دائرة وإهليلجياً ويبطله أي يبطل قول ابن الهيثم ما ذكرناه من أمر الخسوف فإن هذا الاحتمال يقتضي أن لا ينخسف القمر أصلاً والكسوف وقع هذا اللفظ في نسخة الأصل، ولذلك أخر المصنف كلام ابن الهيثم إلى هذا الموضع لكنه ضرب عليه بالقلم آخراً إذ لا ضرب عليه بالقلم آخراً إذ لا وجه لصحته والاعتراض على ما ذكروه بعد تسليم الأصول التي بنوه عليها أن نفي هذا الاحتمال الذي أبداه ابن الهيثم في تشكلات القمر بمنافاته الخسوف لا ينفي جميع العقلية في تلك التشكلات فلعل ثمة سبباً آخر لاختلاف نور القمر مخالفاً لما ذكره وما ذكرتموه لكنا لا نعلمه كأن يكون مثلاً كوكب كمد تحت فلك القمر فينخسف به في بعض استقبالاته غير ما ذكرتم من الخسوف والكسوف ودوام نور باقي الكواكب يجوز أن يكون لخلق الفاعل المختار النور في الشمس والقمر في أكثر الأوقات، وعدم خلقه النور فيهما أحياناً وخلقه إياه في باقي الكواكب دائما أو استضاءتها أي أو لاستضاءة الشمس والقمر والكواكب المحسوسة مطلقاً بكواكب أخر مستورة عنا لا نشاهدها أصلاً وإن كانت مضيئة جداً إما لبعدها أو لكونها محجوبة ببعض الأجرام السماوية المظلمة ثم يتغير الحال فيهما دون باقي الكواكب كيف لا يجوز هذا الاحتمال والحال أن هناك احتمالاً آخر أبعد منه والاحتمالات. قوله: (ولذلك أخر المصنف كلام ابن الهيثم إلى هنا) يعني أن الموضع اللائق بكلامه هو المقصد الثاني في خسوف القمر لكنه ذكره هاهنا لأجل أنه ذكر الكسوف هاهنا لكنه ضرب عليه القلم كما ذكره.
And know that Ibn al-Haytham said concerning the variation of the phases of the moon: It is possible that this variation is because the moon is a luminous sphere, half of it being less than half, and that sphere rotates on its own center with a motion equal to the motion of its sphere. So when its illuminated half faces us, as in opposition, it is full, or the dark half, as in conjunction, it is new, and between them the amount of what we see of the illuminated part varies, being crescent, half-circle, and oblong. And it is refuted, i.e., Ibn al-Haytham's statement is refuted, by what we mentioned concerning lunar eclipse, for this probability would require that the moon not eclipse at all. And the word 'eclipse' occurred in the original copy, and therefore the author delayed Ibn al-Haytham's speech to this place, but he struck it out at the end, because there is no validity to it. And the objection to what they mentioned, after accepting the principles upon which they built it, is that negating this probability which Ibn al-Haytham proposed concerning the phases of the moon because it contradicts lunar eclipse, does not negate all rational explanations for those phases. Perhaps there is another cause for the variation of the moon's light different from what he mentioned and what you mentioned, but we do not know it. For example, a dull planet below the moon's sphere such that it eclipses it in some of its oppositions, other than what you mentioned of lunar and solar eclipses and the permanent light of the other planets. It is possible that it is due to the creation of light in the sun and moon by the free Agent at most times, and His not creating light in them at times, and His creating it in the other planets always, or their illumination, i.e., the illumination of the sun, moon, and all perceived planets by other hidden planets that we do not see at all, even if they are very bright, either due to their distance or because they are veiled by some dark celestial bodies, and then the situation changes for the two (sun and moon) but not for the other planets. How can this probability not be allowed, given that there is another probability more remote than it? And the probabilities. His statement: (And therefore the author delayed Ibn al-Haytham's speech to this place) means that the proper place for his speech is the second purpose concerning lunar eclipse, but he mentioned it here because he mentioned solar eclipse here, but he struck it out as he mentioned.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn al-Haytham's theory that the moon's phases are due to its own rotation with half illuminated, and then refutes it by noting that if his theory were true, lunar eclipses would not occur (because the illuminated half would never be opposite the sun during opposition? The refutation is brief: 'this probability would require that the moon not eclipse at all'). Then an objection is raised: refuting this one probability does not eliminate all possible explanations for the phases. Several alternatives are suggested: a dull planet eclipsing the moon, direct creation of light by God, or illumination from hidden stars. The passage ends with a textual note explaining that the author originally placed this discussion here but later struck it out, as it properly belongs in the lunar eclipse section.

تشكلات القمر
هلال
محاق
بدر

Checkpoint
What phenomenon does the refutation of Ibn al-Haytham's theory rely on?
A) Solar eclipse
B) Lunar eclipse
C) The moon's crescent shape
D) The moon's rotation

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Haytham's theory of lunar phases is refuted because it contradicts the occurrence of lunar eclipses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a plausible idea contradicts what Allah has placed before you as undeniable signs—like the lunar eclipse—what prevents you from immediately abandoning that idea? Examine a belief you hold today that you have never tested against Allah's signs. Will you test it today?
?
Ibn al-Haytham's error was subtle: he assumed rotation could explain phases without checking the eclipse prediction. Where in your life have you accepted a comfortable assumption about your own self-sufficiency or your relationship with Allah that you have not subjected to the reality check of His verses? ?

The Objection and Alternative Explanations for Lunar Phases
Raises an objection and lists alternative explanations for lunar phases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that refuting one explanation for lunar phases does not eliminate all rational possibilities, and you will learn several alternative explanations suggested in the text.

Core Idea
Refuting one theory does not close the door; many other explanations remain possible.

After refuting Ibn al-Haytham's theory, the text presents an objection: the refutation only shows his specific theory is false, but there could be many other causes for the moon's phases. The passage then lists several alternative explanations, including a hidden planet, divine intervention, and hidden stars.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states that disproving one probability does not negate all rational possibilities. Read it carefully.
This is a logical point: refuting one hypothesis does not prove another.
2
List the alternative explanations
The text offers several alternative causes: a dull planet under the moon, direct creation of light by God, hidden stars illuminating the moon, etc. Identify each.
Notice that the alternatives are stated as possibilities (يجوز).
3
Reflect on the method
This objection shows that in theological reasoning, one must consider multiple possibilities. The author rejects Ibn al-Haytham's theory but acknowledges other possibilities remain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The passage lists hidden planets and divine creation as possible causes. Are you willing to accept that there may be realities beyond your perception? How does this affect your trust in God's unseen actions in your daily life?

Fire and Air: Absolute and Relative Light
Defines fire as absolute light and air as relative light, with an objection-response on dryness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers classify fire as absolute light and air as relative light, and why fire is considered dry despite its ease of shaping.

Core Idea
Fire's lightness is absolute, air's is relative; dryness comes from consuming moisture, not from shape resistance.

The philosophers divide the four elements based on their natural motion: absolute light things seek the periphery, relative light things rest just below them. Fire is the prime example of absolute light, air of relative light. But fire's dryness requires careful explanation, since we normally associate dryness with rigidity. The author clarifies this by distinguishing ordinary fire from simple fire.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation silently. Notice the structure: definition of absolute light, then fire, then an objection-response, then definition of relative light and air.
Pay attention to the objection: it challenges the definition of dryness.
2
Identify the two categories
Locate the terms 'خفيف مطلق' (absolute light) and 'خفيف مضاف' (relative light). Understand what each seeks and how they differ.
Absolute light seeks the periphery; relative light seeks to be below absolute light but above the others.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Read the objection: dryness is defined as difficulty in accepting and leaving shapes, but fire is easily shaped. The answer distinguishes ordinary fire (mixed with air) from simple fire at the periphery. Recognize why simple fire is truly dry.
The key is that ordinary fire is not pure; it is overcome by air, making it flexible.
4
Understand air's qualities
Air is described as hot and moist by nature. Its coldness is accidental due to proximity to earth and water. Relate this to its position relative to fire and the other elements.
Air's relative lightness means it is heavy compared to fire but light compared to earth and water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : قول الحكماء في أقسام العناصر
( المتأخرون ) من الحكماء ) على أنها أربعة أقسام خفيف مطلق يطلب المحيط في جميع الأحياز ) أي إذا ترك وطبعه في أي حيز كان من أحياز العناصر المغايرة له كان طالباً للمحيط ( وهي النار وهي حارة بالحس) حرارة شديدة في الغاية، ولذلك كانت طالبة المقعر الفلك ( ويابسة لأنها تفنى الرطوبات ) عن الأجسام الملاقية لها فإن قيل : الست فسرت اليبوسة بعسر قبول الأشكال وتركها والنار (بخلافه لأنها ( سهلة التشكل والترك قلنا ذلك الذي ذكرته إنما هو (فيما من النيران وهي مغلوبة بالهواء) فلذلك كانت سهلة القبول والترك ( فلم قلت : إن النار البسيطة ) التي عند المحيط ( كذلك وخفيف مضاف يقتضي أن يكون تحت النار وفوق الآخرين (وهذا الاقتضاء ( هو خفته المضافة ) إلى العنصرين الآخرين وإن كان ثقيلاً بالنسبة إلى النار وحدها ( وهو الهواء ) وإنه ( حار رطب بالطبع أي لو خُلّي وطبعه لأحس منه بالكيفيتين وكذلك الحال (في) الكيفيات المنسوبة إلى (سائر العناصر وما يعرض (له أي للهواء من البرد) إنما هو ( لمجاورة الأرض) والماء
The first purpose: The statement of the philosophers concerning the divisions of the elements. (The later philosophers) held that they are four divisions: (1) absolute light, which seeks the periphery in all places (i.e., if left to its nature in any place among the places of the other elements, it would seek the periphery), and it is fire, and it is hot by sensation, extremely hot, therefore it seeks the concave of the sphere, and dry because it consumes moistures from bodies contacting it. If it is said: Did you not define dryness as difficulty in accepting and leaving shapes? But fire is contrary to that because it is easily shaped and left. We say: That which you mentioned is only (concerning the fires) that are overcome by air, therefore they are easy in acceptance and leaving. So why do you say that the simple fire (which is at the periphery) is also like that? And (2) relative light, which requires to be below fire and above the others (and this requirement is its relative lightness) relative to the other two elements, although it is heavy relative to fire alone, and it is air, and it is hot and moist by nature, i.e., if left to its nature, it would be perceived with both qualities. And likewise the case for the qualities attributed to the rest of the elements. And what occurs to it (i.e., to air) of coldness is only due to its adjacency to earth and water.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the first purpose of the third section on elements, focusing on the philosophers' classification of the four elements. The author defines the four types: absolute light (fire), relative light (air), absolute heavy, and relative heavy (the latter two are discussed later). For fire, it seeks the periphery, is hot, and dry because it consumes moisture. An objection is raised: dryness is defined as difficulty in changing shape, but fire is easily shaped. The answer distinguishes between ordinary fire (mixed with air) and simple fire at the periphery; only the latter is truly dry. Then air is defined as relative light, hot and moist by nature, and its coldness is due to adjacency to earth and water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fire is dry because it is hot and difficult to shape.
Correction: Fire's dryness is defined as consuming moistures, not difficulty in shaping. Ordinary fire is easily shaped due to mixture with air; simple fire at the periphery is truly dry and resistant to shaping.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is simple fire (at the periphery) considered dry?
A) Because it is hot.
B) Because it consumes moistures.
C) Because it is difficult to shape.
D) Because it seeks the periphery.

Key Takeaway
Fire is absolutely light and dry due to consuming moisture, while air is relatively light and naturally hot and moist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you act with the 'absolute lightness' of simple fire, always seeking the highest and unmoved by distractions, or are you like common fire—easily shaped and redirected by the 'air' of your environment? What specific pressure currently shapes you, and what would it take to return to your true nature?
?
The passage says air is hot and moist by nature but becomes cold due to proximity to earth and water. Where in your spiritual life have you allowed 'coldness' from worldly attachments to override your natural warmth? What step will you take today to move away from that contamination?
?
If dryness (consuming moisture) is fire's active quality, are you consuming or being consumed? In your devotions, do you actively burn away distractions and lesser concerns, or do you let them dampen your fire? Make a concrete commitment to one 'moisture' you will remove starting now.

Light Elements: Fire and Air
Classification and definition of light elements with objection-answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the later philosophers classify the light elements (fire and air) based on absolute and relative lightness, and how they address an objection about fire's dryness.

Core Idea
Light elements seek the periphery; fire is absolutely light, air is relatively light.

The philosophers classify the four elements by their natural motion. Fire, the lightest, moves to the outermost sphere, while air lies below it. In this passage, the author presents the definitions of absolute and relative lightness using fire and air as examples, and defends the claim that fire is dry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection and answer structure.
2
Identify absolute and relative lightness
Find the definitions of 'absolute light' (fire) and 'relative light' (air). Note how they differ: absolute light seeks the periphery among all elements, while relative light is below fire but above others.
Fire is also described as hot and dry, air as hot and moist.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection claims fire is not dry because it easily takes shapes. The response distinguishes ordinary fire (overcome by air) from simple fire at the periphery. Understand why this distinction matters.
This reinforces the definition of dryness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : قول الحكماء في أقسام العناصر. ( المتأخرون ) من الحكماء على أنها أربعة أقسام خفيف مطلق يطلب المحيط في جميع الأحياز أي إذا ترك وطبعه في أي حيز كان من أحياز العناصر المغايرة له كان طالباً للمحيط ( وهي النار وهي حارة بالحس) حرارة شديدة في الغاية، ولذلك كانت طالبة المقعر الفلك ( ويابسة لأنها تفنى الرطوبات ) عن الأجسام الملاقية لها فإن قيل : الست فسرت اليبوسة بعسر قبول الأشكال وتركها والنار (بخلافه لأنها ( سهلة التشكل والترك قلنا ذلك الذي ذكرته إنما هو (فيما من النيران وهي مغلوبة بالهواء) فلذلك كانت سهلة القبول والترك ( فلم قلت : إن النار البسيطة ) التي عند المحيط ( كذلك وخفيف مضاف يقتضي أن يكون تحت النار وفوق الآخرين (وهذا الاقتضاء ( هو خفته المضافة ) إلى العنصرين الآخرين وإن كان ثقيلاً بالنسبة إلى النار وحدها ( وهو الهواء ) وإنه ( حار رطب بالطبع أي لو خُلّي وطبعه لأحس منه بالكيفيتين وكذلك الحال (في) الكيفيات المنسوبة إلى (سائر العناصر وما يعرض (له أي للهواء من البرد) إنما هو ( لمجاورة الأرض) والماء ( وثقيل مطلق يطلب المركز على معنى أنه يقتضي انطباق مركز ثقله على مركز العالم لا على مركز حجمه فإنه لا يقتضي ذلك الانطباق وهو ظاهر مركز الحجم هو أن يكون من جميع الجوانب بالنسبة إليه على السوية، كما مر في بيان مركز الدوائر والكرات ويقتضيه كون ذلك العنصر كرة في وسط الكل كما سيذكره في المقصد الرابع ومركز الثقل هو أن يكون الثقل من جميع الجوانب على السوية بالنسبة إليه ويقتضيه كونه ثقيلاً مطلقاً وهما قد يتحدان كما إذا كانت الكرة متشابهة الأجزاء قد لا يتحدان كما في الكرة التي نصفها من خشب ونصفها من حديد . فهو إذا ترك وطبعه في أي حيز كان من أحياز العناصر المغايرة له طلبه ( وهي الأرض باردة يابسة ويحققهما الحس وثقيل مضاف يقتضي أن يكون فوق الأرض وتحت الآخرين. وهذا الذي ذكرناه هو ( ثقلته المضافة إلى العنصرين الآخرين، وإن كان خفيفاً بالنسبة إلى الأرض وحدها (وهو الماء بارد رطب بالطبع على ما مر من التفسير وطبيعته الجمود لأن طبيعته البرد وإنه يوجب جمودة لكن الشمس تذيبها.
The first aim: The statement of the philosophers on the divisions of the elements. The later philosophers hold that they are four divisions: an absolute light [element] that seeks the periphery in all places, meaning that if it is left to its nature in any place among the places of the elements other than it, it seeks the periphery—and it is fire, and it is hot by sensation, intensely hot in the extreme, and therefore it seeks the concave of the sphere; and dry because it consumes moistures from bodies that come into contact with it. If it is said: Did you not explain dryness as difficulty in receiving shapes and leaving them, while fire is contrary to that because it is easy to shape and leave? We say: That which you mentioned is only in the case of fires that are overcome by air, therefore they are easy to receive and leave. So why did you say that the simple fire at the periphery is likewise? And a relative light [element] necessitates being below fire and above the others—and this necessitation is its relative lightness in relation to the other two elements, even though it is heavy relative to fire alone—and it is air, and it is hot and moist by nature, meaning that if left to its nature, one would sense both qualities from it; and likewise the case with the qualities attributed to the other elements, and what occurs to it (i.e., air) of cold is only due to its proximity to earth and water. And an absolute heavy [element] seeks the center, meaning that it necessitates the coincidence of its center of gravity with the center of the world, not with the center of its volume, for it does not necessitate that coincidence, and that is evident; the center of volume is that from all sides relative to it they are equal, as previously explained in the exposition of the center of circles and spheres, and it is necessitated by that element being a sphere in the middle of the whole, as he will mention in the fourth aim; and the center of gravity is that gravity from all sides is equal relative to it, and it is necessitated by its being absolutely heavy; and they may coincide, as when the sphere is of uniform parts, and may not coincide, as in a sphere half of which is wood and half iron. So if it is left to its nature in any place among the places of the elements other than it, it seeks it—and it is earth, cold and dry, and sensation verifies them; and a relative heavy [element] necessitates being above earth and below the others, and this which we have mentioned is its relative heaviness in relation to the other two elements, even though it is light relative to earth alone—and it is water, cold and moist by nature according to the preceding interpretation, and its nature is solidification because its nature is cold and it necessitates solidification, but the sun melts it.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the four elements according to the later philosophers. It begins by stating that the elements are four: fire, air, water, earth. Each is defined by its relative or absolute lightness or heaviness and by its sensory qualities. Fire is absolutely light, seeking the periphery; it is extremely hot and dry, consuming moisture. An objection is raised that fire is not dry because it easily takes shapes, but the answer distinguishes between ordinary fire (overcome by air) and the simple fire at the periphery, which is dry. Air is relatively light: it is hot and moist by nature, and any coldness it acquires is due to proximity to earth and water. Earth is absolutely heavy, seeking the center, and its center of gravity must coincide with the world's center (not necessarily its center of volume). Water is relatively heavy: cold and moist by nature, tending to solidify but melted by the sun. The passage then defines centers of volume and gravity and notes they may or may not coincide. Finally, it reiterates that each element, when left to its nature, moves to its appropriate place.

خفيف مضاف
حرارة

برودة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fire is not dry because it easily changes shape.
Correction: The dryness of fire refers to its tendency to consume moisture; ordinary fire is influenced by air, but pure fire at the periphery is dry.

Checkpoint
What is the nature of fire according to the philosophers in this passage?
A) Absolutely light, hot, and moist.
B) Absolutely light, hot, and dry.
C) Relatively light, hot, and dry.
D) Absolutely heavy, hot, and dry.

Key Takeaway
Fire is absolutely light and dry, air is relatively light and moist; natural place determines elemental classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers classify fire as absolutely light, always seeking the periphery regardless of its location. In your spiritual life, is your pursuit of Allah absolute—seeking Him in every circumstance—or only when it is convenient or relative to your worldly successes? This lesson defines intrinsic vs. comparative qualities; which describes the nature of your faith?
?
The distinction between pure fire and fire mixed with air shows that mixing can obscure true nature. Are there areas in your life where your actions appear soft or pliable (like fire mixed with air) but your inner core is consumed by selfishness or dryness? What would your 'pure' self look like if all worldly mixture were removed?
?
Air is relatively light—it rises above water and earth but sinks below fire. In your daily interactions, do you seek the 'higher' company of the righteous (like fire) or settle comfortably among those below? This lesson teaches that natural place reveals essence; what does your choice of companions and priorities reveal about your soul's true nature?

Heavy Elements: Earth and Water
Classification and definition of heavy elements with center concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how earth and water are classified as absolutely and relatively heavy, and the distinction between center of volume and center of gravity.

Core Idea
Heavy elements seek the center; earth is absolutely heavy, water is relatively heavy.

After the light elements, the philosophers turn to the heavy ones. Earth, being absolutely heavy, moves to the center of the universe, and the author explains the crucial distinction between the center of volume and the center of gravity. Water, relatively heavy, lies above earth but below the other elements, and its nature is cold and moist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the definitions of absolute and relative heaviness. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
Pay special attention to the discussion of centers.
2
Understand absolute heavy: earth
Earth is absolutely heavy, seeking the center. Note the distinction between center of volume and center of gravity. The example of a sphere half wood half iron illustrates when they differ.
The center of gravity must coincide with the world's center, not necessarily the center of volume.
3
Understand relative heavy: water
Water is relatively heavy: above earth but below air and fire. It is cold and moist by nature, tending to solidify but melted by the sun.
Contrast water's natural coldness with air's heat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that earth's center of gravity must align with the world's center for it to be at rest. Is your life's 'center of gravity' aligned with its true center—your purpose and the truth you know—or are you pulled in directions that leave you off-balance? What one practical change can you make today to recalibrate?
?
Water is described as 'relative heavy'—it settles above earth but below the others. In your relationships, responsibilities, and spiritual life, are you clear about what should be above and what below? Have you inverted priorities, placing what is 'lighter' above what is weightier? Write down one area where you need to restore the proper order.
?
The heterogenous sphere (half wood, half iron) illustrates that the center of volume may differ from the center of gravity. In your own self, what appears balanced on the outside (center of volume) may not reflect your true weight and inclinations (center of gravity). Where do your outward shows not match your inner reality? Commit to one step toward honesty with yourself and with Allah.

Objection and Five Opinions for a Single Element
Objection that elements may be one, with five opinions on its identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the four-element theory and the five alternative opinions proposing a single primordial element.

Core Idea
Five different philosophers argued for a single element, each choosing a different candidate.

The author presents a critical objection to the claim that the elements are four: why could they not be one? He then lists five opinions held by those who assert the elements are one. Each opinion selects a different substance—fire, air, water, earth, or the sea—as the primordial element from which others derive by condensation or rarefaction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a statement about the arrangement of elements, then an objection, then five enumerated opinions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
Find the question: 'Why is it not possible that they are not four?' This is the munaqada (contradiction) that challenges the four-element framework.
3
List the five opinions
The passage lists five opinions for what the one element might be: fire, air, water, earth, and the sea (water again). Observe the rationale given for each.
Each opinion uses a different physical property (simplicity, flexibility, etc.)
4
Understand the derivations
For each opinion, note how the other elements are produced: by condensation (takathuf) or rarefaction (takhalkhul) or rarefaction (taltaf), etc.
5
Reflect on the broader debate
Consider that this objection shows the four-element theory was not universally accepted. The monist alternatives were part of the philosophical discussion.

Source Text

قالوا وعلى الترتيب المذكور تكون العناصر المتناسبة متجاورة والمتضادة كالنار والماء وكالهواء والأرض متباعدة وما كان منها ألطف فهو إلى الفلك أقرب وما كان أكثف فهو أبعد فهذا هو النصف المحكم الذي عليه الوجود قال المصنف: (المناقضة) لما ذكروه أن يقال : (لم لا يجوز أن لا تكون أربعة ؟ بل الحق أحد الأقوال التي ذكرها الآن إذ قيل هي (واحدة) واختلفوا في تلك الواحدة (على خمسة أقوال : الأول : إنما هي النار لشدة بساطتها ) إذ لا جسم أصرف في طبيعته من النار ( إذ الحرارة ( المفرطة التي فيها ( مديرة للكائنات ولأنها تحيل الغير إلى طبعها وحصلت البواقي من النار (بالتكاثف) فهي نار متكاثفة على وجوه متفاوتة. ( الثاني : ) الهواء الرطوبته ومطاوعته للانفعالات ) ولا شك أن الأصل يجب أن يكون مطاوعاً للتغيرات ( ويحصل) من الهواء النار بالحرارة الملطفة فهي هواء لطفته الحرارة ) والباقيان بالبرودة (المكثفة فهما هواء متكاثف تكاثفاً متفاوتاً. ( الثالث : ( هي ( الماء إذ قبوله التخلخل ) بالحرارة ( والتكاثف ) بالبرودة ( محسوس ) فحصل من تخلخله الهواء والنار ومن تكاثفه الأرض. (الرابع ) هي ( الأرض وحصلت البواقي بالتلطيف ( الواقع على مراتب مختلفة (الخامس : ) هي البحار لتوسطه بين الأربعة ) في اللطافة والكثافة فبازدياد لطافته يصير هواء ونارا، وبازدياد كثافته ماء وأرضاً.
They said: And on the mentioned arrangement, the proportionate elements are contiguous, and the contrary ones like fire and water and like air and earth are distant, and whichever among them is more subtle is nearer to the sphere, and whichever is denser is farther. This is the established half on which existence is based. The author said: The contradiction to what they mentioned is that it could be said: Why is it not possible that they are not four? Rather, the truth is one of the opinions that he now mentions, for it is said they are one, and they differed concerning that one on five opinions: The first: It is fire, due to its extreme simplicity, for there is no body purer in its nature than fire, since the excessive heat in it manages the generated things, and because it transforms others into its nature, and the remaining [elements] come from fire by condensation, so they are condensed fire in varying ways. The second: Air, due to its moisture and its compliance to affections, and there is no doubt that the principle must be compliant to changes, and fire comes from air by rarefying heat, so it is air rarefied by heat, and the other two by condensing cold, so they are air condensed in varying degrees. The third: Water, since its acceptance of rarefaction by heat and condensation by cold is perceived, so from its rarefaction come air and fire, and from its condensation comes earth. The fourth: Earth, and the remaining ones come by rarefaction occurring in different degrees. The fifth: The sea (water), due to its intermediacy among the four in subtlety and density, so by increase of its subtlety it becomes air and fire, and by increase of its density it becomes water and earth.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage first states the natural arrangement of the four elements: similar ones are adjacent, contrary ones distant, with lighter elements nearer the sphere. Then it presents an objection (munaqada) to the claim that there are exactly four elements: why could they not be one? The author then lists five opinions held by those who assert a single element. (1) Fire, due to its simplicity and transformative power; the other elements are condensed forms of fire. (2) Air, due to its flexibility; fire is rarefied air, earth and water are condensed air. (3) Water, because it can be rarefied to air and fire, or condensed to earth. (4) Earth, with the others produced by rarefaction. (5) The sea (water), intermediate in subtlety and density, producing the others by increases or decreases. This shows that the philosophers' claim of four is not universally accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a rejection of all elemental theories and is dismissible as irrelevant.
Correction: The objection is not a dismissal but a presentation of alternative monist views from within the same philosophical tradition, showing the diversity of opinion.

Checkpoint
How many opinions are listed for those who say the elements are one?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The debate on the number of elements was not settled; alternative monist theories existed, each grounded in observed properties of the candidate element.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers were deeply convinced by their arguments for a single element. Is there a belief you hold so strongly that you would resist alternative evidence? If you were to examine it with the same logical scrutiny, would it still hold up?
?
The fifth opinion describes the sea as intermediate, capable of becoming fire or earth through subtlety or density. In your spiritual life, are you moving toward greater subtlety (refinement, closeness to God) or becoming denser (worldly attachments)? What specific change can you make today to increase your subtlety?
?
This lesson shows that even within a shared tradition, thinkers disagreed fundamentally. When you encounter someone who holds a deeply different worldview, do you dismiss them or do you genuinely examine the reasoning? What would it take for you to change your mind on a core belief?
